# **INTRODUCTION**

---

## **EXT. MIAMI VALLEY, OHIO – TWILIGHT – DECEMBER 1989**

The land is a flat lung holding its breath. Corn stubble bristles through a crust of old snow, each stalk a rusted pin in the skin of Clark County. The wind comes low and lateral, a farmhand’s shove, carrying road salt and diesel and that wet-iron smell that means winter won’t be pretty about anything. Every fence line draws a staff across the fields; every mile of ditch hums its own bass. You don’t *look* across this place so much as grind your eyes along it. The horizon is a stubborn machine.

To the southwest, beyond the county line, the runways at Wright-Patterson spool out in a sequence of concrete vertebrae. That’s the clock everyone pretends they can’t hear: the Cold War’s metronome, still ticking. Cargo bellies and test birds ride the sky all day, all night, KC tankers, whatever they’re calling the fat ones this year, eternal, indifferent. The sound isn’t dramatic; it’s a backup singer. It's a long, high, uterine drone that gets into your walls, into your teeth, into the way you time your sentences. When the wind is right, the contrails etch white veins into the bruise-colored dusk and the whole county feels like a listening device.

## **EXT. US ROUTE 40 – CONTINUOUS**

Two lanes lay a black ribbon through frost-burned fields. The asphalt has a memory of summer. soft spots where tar bubbled and crows learned the word “shimmer.” Now it spits salt under pickup tires and telephones its little grievances through the suspension. On the shoulders: broken beer bottles that winter hasn’t yet erased, catalogues loosening in their plastic sleeves, a mailbox with the flag up like it’s saluting *anyone* who’ll bother to stop.

Subdivision ghosts hang where the corn gives up: cul-de-sacs with five houses and a dream, model homes facing outward like teeth that never quite bit. There are barns tilting into their own past tense and ranch houses that smell like coffee and damp carpet. The churches come regular as punctuation, each with a signboard sermon doing its level best to outshout the jets. The jets win. They always do.

## **EXT. BETHEL TOWNSHIP – EDGE OF EVENING**

Bethel isn’t a township so much as a triangle. It has a high school that looks like a fortress built by someone who mistrusts teenagers and fluorescent light. It has diners that sell broasted chicken on white plates, not ironic ones. It has a feed store in New Carlisle and a beauty shop in Park Layne and a little municipal building next to the machine shop that has the front of the building painted with a C-141 mural. Mostly, it has edges: the borders of fields, the borders of lives, the line you draw in your head between staying and going.

The ground freezes here in sheets. The kind of cold that isn’t cinematic, no glittering fairy-tale snow, just a wet slap that climbs your jeans by capillary action and sets up a mortgage in your bones. You can see your breath, but it isn’t poetic; it’s proof you’re not dead yet. Knuckles split. Lips chap. Guitar strings bite back harder. The light goes early and refuses to apologize. When the sun drops, the temperature doesn’t fall, it *decides to kill you.*

## **EXT. GILLEY’S DRIVEWAY – MOMENTS LATER**

Gravel, half ice, half memory. A sagging basketball hoop leans over like it’s been listening to arguments for years and still hasn’t picked a side. The ranch house has that winter-tight look, shades drawn, heat hoarded. On the far side: a garage. Square, ordinary, the kind of structure meant for tools and quiet. It has not been informed about its future.

Overhead, another Air Force bird threads the dusk. The sound drops into the driveway and sits there like a big dog nobody invited. The door in the garage buckles at the noise, just a little, just enough to register, and for a second you can mistake the whole county for a drum head. If you were a kid with a snare in your blood, you’d feel it: a phantom kick, the sky doing four-on-the-floor.

## **INT. GILLEY’S PARENTS’ GARAGE – CONTINUOUS – DECEMBER 1989**

Unheated space, concrete floor cold enough to file your bones down. The breath of the room hangs in paper-thin ghosts. Pegboard with its open wounds of missing tools. A rusted mower that won't ever admit defeat. Oily rags that gave up on being clean sometime during the Carter administration. A shop light does its jittery best to play God.

Listen. The jets are still there even inside, just quieter, filtered through siding and insulation and whatever dreams garages are built of. They’re not background. They’re percussion you didn’t hire. A high, persistent tone, the county’s own tinnitus. It makes conversation bend. It makes silence rare.

This is a room that teaches sound its first manners. Everything rings in here: a tap on metal becomes a proclamation; a dropped pick turns into a thesis about gravity. The concrete hands back your mistakes with interest. The air is thick with rubber and old gasoline and someone’s winter coat that never dried right. You can smell plywood. You can smell ambition the way you smell ozone right before lightning.

Ohio has already done its part. The fields flattened you into impatience. The roads taught you endurance. The weather told you you’re not special. The Air Force told you that the loudest thing wins. The only question left is what you do with your hands.

A box fan in the corner (wrong season, wrong tool) sits like a promise. Extension cords spider across the floor. Cases cough up their contents: thrift-store cymbals, pawnshop bass, a synth with fingerprints already tattooed into the keys. The space doesn’t look ready. It *is.*

Outside, a jet climbs and the pitch slides, a steel keening that scratches the roof. Inside, something answers, a tuning note at first, then a second, then the small, brave sound of a stick being tested on a snare. The county’s drone and the human one find each other for a heartbeat and you can hear the hinge turn.

## **EXT. SKY OVER CLARK COUNTY – SAME**

Contrails are chalk marks on a blackboard no one erases. The Cold War isn’t over here yet; it’s just paperwork somewhere else. The pilot in that aluminum cathedral doesn’t know he’s keeping time for six kids about to name their future. He doesn’t have to. The engine writes the downbeat; the ground will supply the rest.

## **INT. GILLEY’S PARENTS’ GARAGE – THE BEGINNING**

The door yawns open, coughing out a rectangle of bruised light. Boots on concrete. The sound of fingers waking up wire. A laugh that’s half challenge, half prayer.

Buckeye dirt underfoot. Jet noise above. Between them: a room that’s about to decide it’s the heart of it all.

---

# **CHAPTER ONE: A GARAGE STORY**

---

## **INT. GILLEY'S PARENTS' GARAGE – LATE AFTERNOON – DECEMBER 1st, 1989**

The garage is a monument to suburban neglect. Tools hang haphazardly, a rusted lawnmower lurks in the corner, and oily rags are strewn across a workbench. The air is thick with the smell of motor oil and stale beer (courtesy of **GILLEY**’s older brother, presumably). A single bare bulb hangs precariously from the ceiling, casting harsh shadows.

**[[4.2 JEREMY GILLEY character bible|GILLEY]]**, all six-foot-four of him, hunched over like a praying mantis in oversized glasses, gestures with a half-empty can of Mr. Pibb.

**GILLEY** 
Gentlemen, and **KATHY**…Behold! Our temple of rock. Or, you know, whatever the hell this is.

He kicks a stack of old newspapers, sending them scattering.

**[[4.1 BILL DAVENPORT character bible|BILL]]** (dryly) 
Looks more like a shrine to your dad’s handyman career.

Davenport, compact and muscular, surveys the mess with disdain. He’s already meticulously wiping down his synthesizer, a gleaming, futuristic beacon in the grime.

**[[4.3 JEFF EVANS character bible|JEFF]]** (pushing up his glasses) 
Function over form, **BILL**. The acoustics in here are…suboptimal.

**JEFF**, hunched over like his friend **GILLEY**, but with a more intense, almost twitchy energy, adjusts his bass.

**[[4.4 JIM BROWN character bible|JIM]]** (grinning, patting his Frankenstein drum kit) 
Suboptimal? Man, this place is perfect! It’s got that raw, primal vibe. Like…like a goddamn cave.

**JIM**, beaming, adjusts a cymbal that looks like it was salvaged from a junkyard.

**KATHY**, dressed in an oversized flannel shirt and ripped jeans, stands awkwardly near the back, clutching a battered guitar case. She avoids eye contact.

**[[4.5 KATHY ESPICH character bible|KATHY]]** (muttering) 
It’s…cozy.

**GILLEY** 
Cozy? Honey, this ain’t no goddamn tea party. This is where we forge legends. This is where we…

He trails off, searching for the right words.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
…where we make some goddamn noise!

Just then, the garage door burst open, revealing **JEREMY EVANS**, breathless. He’s clutching a textbook.

**[[4.6 JEREMY EVANS character bible|JEREMY]]** 
Sorry, sorry! Calculus. Fucking calculus.

**JEFF** (snapping) 
Calculus? We’ve been waiting for half an hour! This ain’t no goddamn study group!

**JEREMY** (wiping sweat from his brow) 
I know, I know. But Mrs. Crabtree…she’s got eyes like a goddamn hawk. And breath that could strip paint.

**JEREMY (V.O.)** Crabtree. That bitch could smell fear from a mile away. And teenage desperation? That was her goddamn perfume.

**BILL** (scoffs) 
Excuses, excuses. Next time, skip the quadratic equations and prioritize the power chords.

**GILLEY** (winking at **KATHY**)
Speaking of power…**KATHY**, you ready to unleash your inner rock goddess?

**KATHY** recoils, pulling her flannel tighter around her.

**KATHY** (mumbling) 
Just…let’s play.

**(CAMERA PUSHES IN ON KATHY’S HANDS, TIGHTLY GRIPPING HER GUITAR. A BEAD OF SWEAT TRICKLES DOWN HER FOREHEAD. THE LIGHT CATCHES THE FAINT OUTLINE OF HER BRA STRAP BENEATH HER SHIRT.)**

**SOUND:** The distorted opening riff of a heavy metal song blasts from Davenport's synthesizer, cutting through the tension.

**BROWN** (counting off wildly) 
One! Two! Three! Four! Let’s fucking do this!

The band erupts into a cacophony. **GILLEY**’s guitar playing is sloppy, **JEFF**’s bass is a rumble, **JIM**’s drums are a chaotic explosion, but Davenport’s synth cuts through it all, a laser beam of pure sound.

**SOUND:** A dog barks furiously from next door, followed by a woman's shrill yell.

**GILLEY** (grinning) 
Sounds like the neighbors are enjoying the show!

**JEFF** (eyes narrowed, lost in the music) 
They’ll be begging for an encore.

**(SLOW MOTION SHOT OF KATHY’S FOOT TAPPING TO THE BEAT. THE CAMERA ZOOMS IN ON HER BOOT, THEN CUTS TO A CLOSE UP OF HER FACE. A SMALL, ALMOST IMPERCEPTIBLE SMILE PLAYS ON HER LIPS.)**

**VOICEOVER (KATHY):** That’s when it started. Not the band, not really. But something inside me. Something…dangerous.

**(CLOSE UP ON GILLEY, HIS FACE RED WITH EFFORT, HIS GLASSES SLIGHTLY CROOKED. HE LOOKS DIRECTLY AT THE CAMERA, A WILD, ALMOST MANIC LOOK IN HIS EYES.)**

**GILLEY (V.O.)** 
We were young. We were loud. And we were gonna fuckin’ conquer the world. Or at least Bethel Township.

**SOUND:** The music crashes to a halt. The band members stand panting, the air thick with sweat and raw energy.

**(THE CAMERA PANS ACROSS THE GARAGE, LINGERING ON EACH BAND MEMBER’S FACE. EACH FACE IS ILLUMINATED BY THE SINGLE BARE BULB, CREATING A SERIES OF HARSH, UNFLATTERING PORTRAITS. THE CAMERA STOPS ON KATHY, WHO IS LOOKING DIRECTLY AT THE LENS, HER EXPRESSION UNREADABLE.)**

**FADE TO BLACK.**

---

## **MONTAGE: THE RISE OF COMMUTED (DECEMBER 1989 – MEMORIAL DAY WEEKEND 1990)**

**SOUNDTRACK:** A distorted, feedback-laden guitar chord explodes, then cuts abruptly to silence. Then a single, insistent drumbeat begins, building in intensity.

**VISUALS:** Quick cuts: a close-up of a frozen puddle in the driveway outside the garage followed by a shot of the band's breath misting in the cold air inside. Then a close up of **GILLEY’s** fingers fumbling on the guitar strings. He looks frustrated.

---

## **WINTER’S GRIP (DECEMBER 1989 – FEBRUARY 1990)**

**SOUNDTRACK:** A bass drum thuds from inside the garage, half-muffled by the snow outside. A guitar pick scrapes against frozen strings—metal against ice, stubborn and sharp.

**VISUALS:** The band is a bundle of coats, hats, and defiance. They heave an engine block across the driveway. It’s not theater, it's machinery, a dare to gravity, Gilley’s ritual of macho spectacle. Jeremy Evans comes running late, skidding on black ice. His books explode across the pavement, pages splaying like wounded birds. He crouches, red-faced, clawing them back together. From the kitchen window, Mr. Gilley nurses a coffee, expression unreadable. He watches, never lifts a hand, never opens his mouth.

**CHARACTER SIGNALS:** Gilley has begun wearing leather jackets indoors, swagger turned costume. Jeremy’s lateness calcifies into a vow. He drills his fingers raw on the fretboard, practicing until obsession becomes armor. Jeff adopts fingerless gloves like a permanent accessory, even when heat hums from the radiators. Jim ties bandanas around his head to corral his hair, sweat and stubbornness mingling under cloth.

**TOUCHSTONE IMAGE:** Kathy’s hands, raw and cracked. She twists the tuning pegs with stiff fingers, each turn biting deeper into split skin. A cut blooms on her index finger. The camera holds—lingering, unblinking—on the wound, on the cost.

---

## **THE THAW (MARCH 1990 – APRIL 1990)**

**SOUND:** Snowmelt drips steady from the gutters. Birds test out weak chirps. Guitars creep from hesitant strum to something resembling confidence.

**VISUALS:** The garage door cracks open for the first time in months, a thin line of sunlight cutting the gloom. The band plays louder, faster, as if the thaw gave them permission.  Gilley’s got a bad perm now, strutting with a microphone, sunglasses on indoors like he’s already famous. He pulls faces at Kathy, crude and suggestive. She keeps her eyes on the concrete floor, tuning him out.

Jeff has started haunting the garage in a trench coat and his own pair of indoor shades. Jim hides under a wool cap and a beard that makes him look older than he is. Jeremy has stopped fingerpicking, he’s switched to a pick, sharp and precise, digging for volume.

**CHARACTER SIGNALS:** Gilley’s ego balloons; he mouths off at school, brags about the band, starts hitting on girls he has no chance with. Jeremy’s playing sharpens fast, but he shrinks from everything else, his world narrowing to six strings. Jeff watches him with quiet hostility, rivalry taking root. Jim can’t stop fiddling with his kit, endlessly tuning, repositioning, chasing a sound he can’t quite describe.

**TOUCHSTONE IMAGE:** Mr. Gilley leans against his car outside, cigarette dangling, the hood propped up more as cover than task. His eyes aren’t on the engine. They’re locked on the garage, on his son’s strut. He takes a long drag, squints, says nothing.

---

## **SUMMER’S HEAT (MAY 1990 – MEMORIAL DAY WEEKEND)**

**SOUND:** Bees hum outside. Inside, amps roar and drums pound. The band finally locks into something loud and whole—more racket than music, but it clicks.

**VISUALS:** The garage door yawns open, full sunlight flooding the clutter. Gilley’s shirtless, a bandanna cinched around his perm, arms scrawled with temporary tattoos that smear in sweat. His sneer looks glued on.  Jeremy closes his eyes mid-solo, hair grown out to cover the thin patches, chasing notes he barely controls.  Jeff’s bare chest gleams under a leather vest, hair slicked back in a half-successful imitation of menace.  Jim has cut the sleeves off every shirt he owns, wrists bound tight in sweatbands.  Kathy’s uniform is black-on-black now. Cigarette in hand, lipstick dark as tar, scowl permanent. She smokes between chords, and plays harder than any of them expect.

**CHARACTER SIGNALS:** The music’s tighter, heavier, and their egos inflate to match. They strut like they’re headlining stadiums, though they’ve never left the garage. Kathy’s guitar snarls louder, her confidence cutting through; she starts talking back to Gilley, biting and sharp. He answers by pretending she isn’t there.

**TOUCHSTONE IMAGE:** Slow motion—Jim’s stick crashing into a cymbal, light exploding off brass. His face caught in open-mouthed ecstasy.  Cut to Mr. Gilley in the shadows, cigarette ember bright in the dark. A thin smile flickers at his mouth.

**SOUND:** The band slams into crescendo. The noise cuts—abrupt silence.

**VISUAL:** They stand, drenched and panting, staring at each other like they’ve just accomplished something monumental.

**FINAL SHOT:** Mr. Gilley steps out into the driveway, framed by sunlight. He watches, shakes his head once, quiet disapproval stamped on his face. He turns, walks back into the house. The garage door stays wide open.

---

## **EXT. TECUMSEH HIGH SCHOOL PARKING LOT – DAY – MEMORIAL DAY WEEKEND 1990**

Car horns blare. Parents cheer too loud. Mortarboards thump against asphalt. The air reeks of sunscreen, hairspray, and cheap perfume. Chrome gleams on dented Chevys, the smell of exhaust rolling over everything. Small-town graduation, loud and ordinary.

**JEREMY GILLEY** stands in the middle of it, drowning in regalia—ropes, medals, sashes. The kid is a walking trophy case. Brilliant. Decorated. Already forgotten. His mop of hair is styled wrong, gelled like an apology.

The band crowds him, rowdy but protective.

**BILL**, stuffed into his gown like it’s about to rip at the seams, slugs Gilley’s arm.

**BILL**
Well, I’ll be damned. Little Einstein survived.

**JEFF**, rare smile curling his mouth, adjusts his glasses. His eyes harden again before anyone can read him.

**JEFF**
Against all odds, he escaped Tecumseh High. Barely scorched.

Gilley grins, shrugs under the weight of medals.

**GILLEY**
Good teachers. Better distractions.

He looks at **KATHY**, crossing the lot in a floral dress that doesn’t erase the black lipstick or the oversized shades. She walks lighter, shoulders straighter—more herself, not less.

**KATHY**
Congrats, Gilley.

**GILLEY** (blushing)
Thanks. Couldn’t have done it without…all of you.

A beat.

**JEFF** fidgets, his fingers working the collar of his shirt. He blurts it before he can stop himself.

**JEFF**
That…dress. It’s…good.

Kathy hides behind her shades, smiles anyway.

**KATHY**
Thanks. It’s new.

Their hands almost touch, Jeff adjusting his glasses, Kathy tugging her bra strap. Almost. Then both pull back, guilty at nothing.

Gilley slaps Jeremy’s back, shattering the moment.

**GILLEY**
Enough mush. Tonight we celebrate.

**JIM**, ZZ Top beard in full bloom, cavalry slouch hat tilted low, yells across the lot.

**JIM**
Party at mine! Dad’s gone!

**JEREMY**
Jim’s dad is out of town? Jackpot!  Let's torch the night.

The boys holler, arms around each other. Flashbulbs pop. They pose stupid in their gowns. Gilley tries to sling an arm around Kathy and she shrugs him off without looking.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
She played hard. They all did. Didn’t matter. They always came around.

Quick cut: Kathy rolling her eyes, lips quirking into the smallest smile.

---

## **MONTAGE – CELEBRATION**

- The band piling into Jim’s rust-scarred van, blasting cassette tapes until the speakers distort.
- Roaring through town, leaning out windows, horns blaring at classmates.
- Gas station stop—sweaty, broke, Gilley tossing junk food and sodas onto the counter like a king.
- Jim’s house strung with balloons and streamers, music already leaking into the night.

**SOUNDTRACK:** Guitars over laughter, snatches of voices too fast to catch. The noise of youth boiling over.

---
## **INT. JIM’S KITCHEN – NIGHT – MEMORIAL DAY WEEKEND 1990**

The party roars down the hall—music bleeding, voices cracking—but the kitchen is quiet except for the hum of the fridge. Empty bottles line the counter.

**JEFF** and **KATHY** are alone. He’s leaning against the sink, trying to talk, words catching in his throat.

**JEFF**
So…you’re…going to college, right?

**KATHY**
Clark Tech. Just for now.

**JEFF** nods too quickly, glasses sliding down his nose.

**JEFF**
Practical. That’s…smart.

Silence stretches. He blurts out:

**JEFF (CONT’D)**
I like your hair.

Kathy touches it, half-smile, half-defensive.

**KATHY**
Thanks.

Jeff almost reaches for a strand, stops short, hand hanging in the air before retreating.

**SOUND:** Music from the living room swells, drowning them out.

**VISUAL:** Jeff’s hand tightening on the rim of the counter. Kathy fidgets with her purse strap. Their hands almost meet in the space between them, then recoil.

---

## **EXT. JIM’S BACK DECK – SUNSET**

The band sprawls on the deck, sweaty gowns shed, plastic cups in hand. They laugh too loud, faces flushed in the last light of day.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
We thought we were unstoppable. Just kids with amps and noise. On top of the world.

**FREEZE FRAME:** Silhouettes against the burning sky, holding still on a moment they won’t get back.

---

## **MONTAGE — MIDWEST SUMMER, 1990**

**SOUNDTRACK:** Crickets chirp. A Reds broadcast bleeds from cheap radios—crowd roar, the patter of Joe Nuxhall on play-by-play, who sounds like your favorite uncle. Guitars sneak in under it, humid and a little out of tune.

---

**EXT. RIVERFRONT PARKING LOT – DAY**
Tailgaters in Reds caps flip burgers on dented grills. A kid chalks a diamond onto hot asphalt. A radio cracks: **“And this one belongs to the Reds!"** The cheer spills over the Ohio River and into every open window in town.

---

**INT. COUNTRY CONNECTION RESTAURANT KITCHEN – NIGHT**
Steam, fryer hiss, ticket rail jammed. **GILLEY** wears an apron like armor, expo’s the line with a drill sergeant’s cadence.  He reroutes chaos—fires the grill, swaps stations, plates dinner with enthusiasm. The owner watches, impressed and terrified. Gilley doesn’t strut here; he **runs** it.  A battered radio near the ice machine burbles Nine Inch Nails between orders.

---

**EXT. BEER TRUCK / ALLEYS & BAR BACKDOORS – DAWN**
**JEREMY** rides shotgun with a broken down delivery driver that reports to his Dad, **Mr. Evans**. hands raw on the two-wheeler. Kegs thud, cases rattle, sweltering alley steam.  Old bartenders nod. Jeremy learns the angle, keep the weight low, count the steps, don’t get crushed.  Between stops, the truck radio fills the cab with traffic and weather together.

---

**INT. FIRST NATIONAL BANK – AFTERNOON**
Fluorescents hum. **KATHY** behind glass, nameplate crooked, lipstick perfect. “Next.”  Rubber stamps. Deposit slips. Crumpled singles. A farmer’s hands shake as he slides a check under the slot; she steadies him with a neat stack of twenties and a small, real smile.

**JEFF** appears two people back in line, sweat-dark shirt, eyes on the floor. When it’s his turn, their fingers **almost** touch over the bills. They don’t.

---

**INT. WRIGHT STATE REGISTRAR – MORNING**
Paper forms, plastic chairs, dot-matrix printers chewing tractor feed. **JIM** signs up for classes with a stubby pencil, then stares too long at the tuition total.  He pockets a student ID, rides the bus back down to K Lot, and disappears into the dreams to retune his snare.

---

**INT. GUITAR SHOP – LATE AFTERNOON**
**BILL** tests a chorus pedal through a beat-up amp, eyes half-closed, listening for the seam where hiss turns pretty.  A well-dressed man his father’s age lingers, friendly, **interested**. Compliments the hands, the ears.  Bill catches it, gives a kind nod, and lets the moment drift. He stacks the pedal back on the shelf like a prayer he can’t afford. Music first. Everything else is bollocks.

---

**INT. RESTAURANT WALK-IN / EXT. GAS STATION / INT. BANK BREAK ROOM – VARIOUS**
Reds on every radio: in Gilley’s walk-in cooler over boxed lettuce; in Jeremy’s truck while Mr. Evans counts empties; in Kathy’s break room above a vending machine that eats quarters.  Same voice. Same summer heartbeat.

---

**EXT. JIM’S BACKYARD – NIGHT**
The band slumps in plastic chairs, grease on their hands, timecards in their pockets. Mosquitoes whine. Someone fans themselves with a Wright State pamphlet.  From a neighbor’s radio: crowd noise spikes—**And this one belongs to the Reds!**  Nobody cheers. They just breathe a little easier in the dark.

**VISUAL:** Close on small things: tip calluses on Jeremy’s fingers, Kathy’s ink-smudged teller stamp, Gilley’s order tickets clipped in perfect rows, Jim’s student ID bent at the corner, Bill’s thumbnail nicked from a stubborn string.

**SOUND:** Crickets again. Then the garage door rolls up. Amps hum to life. The radio stays on in the corner, baseball under everything, as the first chord punches the heat.

---

## **LATE-NIGHT REHEARSALS (JULY 1, 1990)**

**SOUND:** Drums hammer, bass thumps, guitars screech and stumble into something sharper. The neighbor’s dog answers like it’s part of the band.

**VISUAL:** The garage is a sauna under a single bare bulb, sweat dripping onto concrete. Jeremy stomps a new effects pedal, layering echoes that almost sound professional. Gilley thrashes through the same three chords, sunglasses slipping, jaw clenched. Jim pounds away on a kit so oversized it swallows the room. Kathy’s in her leather jacket, black against heat, guitar snarling louder than anyone expects.

---

**GILLEY** (shouting over the noise)
Jesus, Jeremy, you trying to strangle that guitar? Sounds like roadkill in a blender!

**JEREMY**
It’s called delay. You wouldn’t know it if it bit you.

**JIM** (grinning)
Nah, man—it’s sick. Like Pink Floyd, if they were pissed off.

**KATHY**
More like Pink Fraud, Jer!

**JEFF** (to Jeremy, deadpan)
At least you’re evolving. Some of us are still stuck on caveman chords.

**GILLEY**
Fuck you, Jeff. Cavemen built the world.

**JIM**
Yeah, and then they died at twenty-three.

Laughter bursts, mean and electric. The ribbing keeps coming, nobody safe—Bill for flexing too hard, Kathy for scowling through solos, Jim for needing six toms to play a four-beat. The insults sting, but the music keeps tightening.

---

**TOUCHSTONE IMAGE:** Cut to **MR. GILLEY**, sitting in the living room with a newspaper limp in his hands. The garage noise rattles the walls, leaks through the screen door.

He lowers the paper, listening harder than he wants to admit. Not pride—something more cautious.

**MR. GILLEY (V.O.)**
They’re raw. They’re a mess. But…hell. Maybe there’s something there.

---

## **CLEANUP (AUGUST 1990)**

**SOUND:** The garage cools into clatter: cymbals stacked, broom bristles scraping concrete, ice cubes chiming into plastic cups of lemonade.

**VISUAL:** The band moves like clockwork now. Amps rolled back, cords coiled, drum kit folded in on itself. They’ve practiced this drill so many times it’s muscle memory. Chaos in, chaos out. The garage transforms from furnace back to storage shed in minutes.

**JEFF** zips around like a puppy—dragging cables, stacking milk crates, trying to be everywhere at once. He knocks a broom into **KATHY’s** hand, then grabs another so they sweep side by side. This time, when their hands brush, neither jerks away. Just a quick, crooked smile passed between them.

On the workbench, **GILLEY** slouches, arms crossed, watching with a sour edge. The band is fast, efficient, together. He looks like the only one sitting out the rhythm.

**TOUCHSTONE IMAGE:** Close on **KATHY**’s hand swiping sweat from her forehead. She catches **JEFF** staring—puppy-eyed, worshipful. He snaps his gaze down to the broom, suddenly very busy.

**SOUND:** A broom skids into the corner. Someone exhales hard. The garage door slams shut. Silence presses in.

---

## **THE NOD (AUGUST 1990)**

**SOUND:** The scrape of wood legs against concrete. Grunts. Labored breaths. A TV drifts in from the house, a flat, sonorous newscaster voice: *“President Bush has ordered U.S. forces to Saudi Arabia in response to Iraq’s invasion of Kuwait…”* The words trail into static as a cymbal crashes into its case.

**VISUAL:** The band heaves the workbench back toward the open driveway. It’s a beast—heavier, more stubborn than the day they dragged it in. Sweat drips, arms shake, curses fly, but the movements are practiced now. Cords are coiled, amps lifted, the teardown slick and fast, a routine hammered in night after night.

**MR. GILLEY** stands in the driveway, arms folded tight, hawk-eyed. He watches every detail: Jeremy angling the corner, Bill bracing with his shoulder, Jeff darting around with nervous energy, Kathy shoving through the strain, Jim barking counts like a sergeant.

The workbench finally lurches over the threshold and slams onto the asphalt. The kids pant, bent double, skin shining with sweat.

**CHARACTER SIGNAL:** For the briefest beat, Mr. Gilley’s chin dips—just once. A nod. Barely visible, but there. Acknowledgement that they’re starting to function like a unit.

**FINAL SHOT:** The band straightens, standing in the driveway under humid twilight, chests heaving, listening to the muffled voice of the TV inside: *“…tens of thousands of troops expected in the coming weeks…”*

They look at Mr. Gilley. He gives nothing else—no smile, no word—just turns and disappears into the house. The nod lingers, heavier than the workbench.

## **EXT. SUBURBAN NEIGHBORHOOD – MORNING – LABOR DAY WEEKEND 1990**

The sun crushes everything. Asphalt shimmers, lawns buzz with mowers, kids scream in chlorinated pools, the smell of charred burgers and lighter fluid drifts heavy. Norman Rockwell on the outside. Pressure cooker on the inside.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – MORNING**

The garage is unbearable—air trapped, amps humming heat, sweat dripping off forearms onto warped plywood. The band stumbles through the same song they’ve been grinding for weeks.

**SOUND:** A mangled chord progression collapses mid-bar. Silence, except for ragged breathing.

**GILLEY** (snarling, voice cracked)
Jesus Christ, Davenport, do it again! You can’t keep time to save your sorry ass!

He hurls his pick into the corner. His face is blotched red, eyes rimmed in sweat and fury.

**BILL** (throws his hands up)
Maybe if you weren’t chasing virtuoso bullshit, we could actually finish a song. You think this is Bon Jovi? Wake the fuck up.

**GILLEY** (spits)
Fun? You call your toy-keyboard horseshit fun? Nobody’s buying your Casio symphonies, Bill.

**JEFF** (quiet, shaky)
Five minutes. Just five. I can’t breathe in here.

**JIM** (slamming the snare)
Yeah, sure, let’s all go watch cartoons. Fuck it. We’re never gonna make it out of this garage anyway.

**KATHY** (erupts, voice ragged)
Shut the fuck up, every one of you! We are this close!

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
Close? We were close to killing each other.

They launch back into the riff. Two bars in—

**GILLEY** (screaming at Jeremy)
You’re dragging! You always drag! You play like a fucking snail!

**JEREMY** (fists clenched, trembling)
At least I can play. You? You’re a two-chord joke.

**JEFF** (turns on Kathy, eyes sharp behind his glasses)
And you, you’re barely here. Wrong notes, dead stare. You’re a fucking ghost, Kathy.

**JIM** (joins in, bitter)
Yeah. You’re why we sound like shit. You’re dead weight.

Kathy breaks, shoulders shaking, eyes wet, rage and humiliation spilling together.

**KATHY** (cracking)
At least I’m not a talentless, egotistical, insecure—

The words snag in her throat.

**JEFF** (spits, venom)
Say it! A cunt? That’s what you were gonna say? Because that’s what you are.

**VISUAL:**
Tight on Kathy’s face, a tear streaks down, mascara smudging in the heat. Cut to Jeff, rage already souring into regret.

**KATHY** (screaming, breaking)
Fine! I quit!

She slams her guitar case into the bench, storms out into the blinding sunlight. The garage door gapes open behind her.

The band freezes. Sweat drips. Nobody moves.

**GILLEY** (flat, hollow)
You’re worse than a bunch of goddamned animals.

From the shadows: **MR. GILLEY**, arms crossed. He’s been there the whole time. His face is stone, disappointment carved deep. He shakes his head once, turns for the house. This time he pauses at the doorway, looks back at the wreckage of kids and instruments, then vanishes inside.

**SOUND:** Lawnmowers drone. Kids laugh next door. A screen door slams. The world goes on.

## **INT. GILLEY KITCHEN – CONTINUOUS**

**KATHY** bursts into the kitchen, a whirlwind of anger and hurt. The kitchen is clean and functional, a stark contrast to the chaotic garage. Sunlight streams through the window, illuminating the dust motes dancing in the air. She heads straight for the sink, turning the cold water on full blast, splashing it on her face. Her breath comes in ragged gasps.

She searches for a glass, her movements jerky and impatient. Not finding one, she’s about to cup her hands to drink when **MR. GILLEY** appears in the doorway. He’s not imposing, but his presence fills the room. He’s wearing a simple work shirt and jeans, his face etched with the lines of hard work and quiet observation. He silently offers her a glass filled with ice water.

**SOUND:** The rush of the faucet, **KATHY**’s heavy breathing, the clinking of ice in the glass. The distant drone of a lawnmower from outside.

**KATHY** (voice choked with emotion) 
Thanks.

She takes the glass, her hand trembling slightly, and gulps down the water. The coldness soothes her burning throat.

**MR. GILLEY** (His voice low and steady) 
You alright?

**KATHY** looks up, surprised. This isn't the usual **MR**. **GILLEY**, the quiet, almost invisible man who blends into the background. His eyes, usually distant, are trained on her with an unexpected intensity.

**KATHY** (wiping her face with the back of her hand) 
Yeah. Fine. Just…frustrated.

She looks away, unable to meet his gaze.

**MR. GILLEY** (quietly, but with a firmness that commands attention) 
Don’t let them…define you.

**KATHY (V.O.)** 
It wasn’t just about the band. It was about everything. About being the only girl, about being treated like one of the guys, about feeling like I was never good enough. He saw that. He saw right through me.

**KATHY** (looking back at him, her voice barely a whisper) 
They…they said some pretty awful things.

**MR. GILLEY** (his gaze unwavering) 
Words are just…blather. It’s what you do that matters.

**MR. GILLEY** (he walks to the counter, grabs a towel, and starts wiping down a spot.) 
You ever seen a hawk hunt?

**KATHY** (shaking her head) 
No, sir.

**MR. GILLEY** (he looks out the window, his gaze distant.) 
Hawks don’t waste breath. They circle. They watch. They wait. Then they strike.

**(VISUAL):** Close-up on **MR. GILLEY’s** hand as he clenches the rag, then slowly releases it. The camera then cuts to **KATHY’s** face, her eyes wide with understanding.

**MR. GILLEY** (turning back to **KATHY**, his voice softer now) 
Go make something of yourself. Show them…show everyone…what you’re capable of.

**KATHY** freezes, his words hitting her like a physical blow. She’s never heard him speak to her like this, with such directness, such belief.

**KATHY** (Her voice trembling) 
Yes, sir.

She sets the glass down on the counter, her movements now deliberate, purposeful. She looks at **MR. GILLEY** one last time, determination burning in her eyes.

**KATHY (V.O.)** 
That was it. That was the moment. Not the practices, not the gigs we wanted to play, not the fights. That conversation…that was the birth of Commuted. It wasn’t about the music anymore. It was about something bigger. It was about proving them wrong. Proving him right.

She turns and walks out of the kitchen, not back to the garage, but towards the front door. She walks out into the bright sunlight, a new path opening before her.

**(FINAL SHOT):** **MR. GILLEY** stands in the kitchen, watching her go. A faint, almost imperceptible smile plays on his lips. He then turns and walks out of the kitchen, back into the house.

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

Kathy circles in from the backyard, her anger cooled into something harder. She steps back into the furnace of the garage.

The band is mid-song, but the sound is limp: Jeff staring at his frets, sloppy; Jim drumming like he’d rather be anywhere else; Gilley leaning against an amp, sullen; Jeremy the only one still grinding with intent.

**SOUND:** Flat notes, muffled and uninspired.

Kathy strides to the mic stand, pulls it to her height. She doesn’t yell—her voice slices the room clean.

**KATHY**
Stop.

The music dies. Eyes snap to her.

**JEFF** (mutters)
What now?

**KATHY** (to Jeff, steady)
You think you can call me that?

Jeff looks down, shame heavy.

**JEFF**
I didn’t mean—

**KATHY**
You did. That’s the problem.

She sweeps her gaze over the rest, Bill, Jeremy, Jim, Gilley, making them hold it.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
We’re not a band. We’re whiny little boys. Whiny, cruel, lazy. No decency. No respect.

**SOUND:** A fly buzzing in circles, laughter from a neighbor’s yard.

**KATHY** (her voice sharpens, rising)
We’re going to do this right. Harder than we ever have. We’re going to be the best thing this town’s ever seen. And if you’re not on board? Leave.

She stares them down.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
This isn’t a democracy. This is a dictatorship. And I’m the dictator.

Silence. Nobody answers. Their faces twist—sour, resentful, teeth clenched—but no one walks. The sweat boils with what they don’t say.

Kathy doesn’t stop.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
Now. From the top.

They fall back in, the same song, but sharper, meaner, driven by her fire.

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – AFTERNOON**

The heat has only gotten worse. Sweat drips down from the rafters, amplifiers radiate like furnaces, the air smells of metal, stale beer, and scorched dust. The band is stripped to the waist, drenched and half-spent. Kathy is still in her tank top, damp and clinging.

The song collapses mid-bar. Kathy rips off her guitar strap and barks.

**KATHY**
Fuck it's hot!

She looks around at their bare torsos—Jeff bent over his bass, Jim red-faced behind the kit, Jeremy wiping sweat from his arm with his shirt, Bill flexing without noticing, Gilley preening.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
Seriously? You’re all half naked, and I’m supposed to sit here roasting in this?

Nobody answers.

Kathy inhales sharply, grabs the hem of her tank, and yanks it off in one motion. She throws it to the concrete, her bare chest shining with sweat, chin lifted in defiance.

**VISUAL:**
The band freezes. Jeff’s eyes dart away instantly, cheeks blazing. Jeremy stares, mouth slack. Jim grips his sticks like a lifeline, transfixed. Bill blinks, trying and failing to act unimpressed. Gilley’s expression sharpens, shifting from shock to anger to something harder—reluctant respect.

**KATHY** (flat, daring them)
What? Never seen tits before? Get over it.

She slings her guitar back on, strums a jagged chord, and barks into the room:

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
Now play.

The boys fall back in, sloppy at first, then pulled into her tempo, into her authority. The air hums with something new—fear, awe, obedience.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – LATER MORNING**

The thunderheads are building outside, the air even heavier. The band is slumped, drained from Kathy’s iron pace. Sweat drips steady to the floor.

**SOUND:** Drip, breath, the distant growl of storm.

The side door creaks. **MR. GILLEY** steps in from the house, a cardboard box in his arms. Inside: popsicles. Red, blue, green, orange, bright against the gloom.

He sets the box down on a bench, wordless, and starts handing them out.

**MR. GILLEY**
Here.

Bill takes one, muttering thanks. Jim bites into his like a man starved. Jeremy stammers out an embarrassed “Appreciate it, sir.” Gilley takes his without a word, not meeting anyone’s eye.

Mr. Gilley stops at Kathy. He holds out a red one. His gaze flickers, steady and unflinching, then he moves on. No judgment. No leer. Just equal.

**KATHY (V.O.)**
He didn’t judge. Didn’t sneer. Didn’t see a freak. He just…saw me. Like I belonged. Like I was one of them.

Mr. Gilley’s voice cuts the silence.

**MR. GILLEY**
Storm’s coming. Best put everything away.

The band stirs. Jeff fumbles for his glasses, Kathy wipes her brow, Jim starts breaking down cymbals, Jeremy coils cords. They move in practiced sync, popsicles clenched between lips, working quickly and efficiently.

Mr. Gilley watches from the doorway, arms crossed, rain beginning to spatter on the roof.

**MR. GILLEY**
Well done.

He turns, disappears into the house. The rain comes harder, steady against the tin roof.

**VISUAL:** Kathy licks her popsicle, bare chest glistening, a look of iron setting in her eyes. The boys glance at her, then down, then back to their work. The dynamic has shifted. Permanently.  Perhaps.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S GARAGE / SNIDER ROAD – AFTERNOON**

The rain has passed. Puddles shine under a gray sky. Steam rises from the asphalt.

**JEFF** and **KATHY** step out of the garage, sweat plastering their hair. She’s still bare-chested, tank top abandoned on the floor inside. Jeff wipes his brow, avoiding her eyes.

**JEFF** (awkward)
Well…that was…something.

**KATHY** (flat)
Yeah. Something.

She glances at his chest, then away. He can’t look at her at all.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
You giving me a ride or what?

**JEFF** (startled)
Oh. Right. Yeah. Sure. Escort’s over there.

He gestures at the faded blue Ford Escort. His keys jangle in his hand, fumbling. She watches him with faint amusement, arms crossed, waiting.

---

## **INT. FORD ESCORT – CONTINUOUS**

The car coughs alive, damp upholstery smelling of mildew. Windows fog at the edges.

Jeff sits stiff, both hands welded to the wheel, eyes locked on the road. Kathy leans back, arms still crossed over her chest, topless, staring out the passenger window.

**KATHY (V.O.)**
He was crawling out of his skin. I could feel it. I wasn’t about to cover up. Not anymore. If he couldn’t handle it, that was on him.

**SOUND:** Engine rumble, tires hissing over wet pavement.

**JEFF** (strained)
So…uh…how’s your summer been?

**KATHY**
Hot. Like always.

**JEFF** (nods too fast)
Yeah. Real hot. Record-breaking, maybe.

**KATHY** (sidelong)
You spend all day shirtless in that oven of a garage. Funny how uncomfortable you look now.

Jeff flushes red, gripping the wheel tighter.

**JEFF**
I’m just…focused on driving.

**KATHY** (smiles faintly)
Right. Driving.

Silence swells. The Escort rattles down National Road, neither looking at the other, both aware of every second stretching long.

---

## **INT. JEFF’S CAR – MOVING – DUSK**

The late-summer light lays gold across the cracked dashboard. Heat presses down, trapped in vinyl and nerves. **JEFF** grips the wheel at ten and two like he’s chauffeuring the Pope.

**KATHY** sits with arms folded across her chest, legs crossed at the ankle. Sunglasses stay on even though the sun’s nearly gone.

The silence isn’t tense. It’s charged.

**JEFF** (too casual)
Think I’ve got a slow leak. Front driver’s side. Back at the air pump every couple weeks. Probably a nail. Or a curse.

**KATHY** (still facing the window)
Could be sabotage. Piss off any tire witches lately?

**JEFF** (grinning)
Just the usual coven at the hardware store. They hide the 3/8 bolts. Ritual sacrifice.

A beat.

**KATHY**
I need brakes. Mine squeal like a pig every time I roll through a drive-thru.

**JEFF**
Pads or rotors?

**KATHY**
Like I’d know. I just turn up the radio.

**JEFF** (proud)
Did my own oil change last week. Took three tries to find the filter, but it’s done.

**KATHY**
Jiffy Lube did mine. Came with a free soda and judgment.

They chuckle. The road hums.

**KATHY**
You still at the hardware store?

**JEFF**
Yeah. Weirdly peaceful. Organizing nails by gauge, showing old men where duct tape lives. Retail Zen.

**KATHY**
The bank’s the opposite. Fluorescents, fake smiles, and middle-aged women who think I’m either a threat or a failure.

Jeff glances at her. The sunglasses don’t give him anything back.

**JEFF**
You’re not a failure.

**KATHY**
I know.

A pause.

**JEFF**
I mean—you’re too smart for Clark Tech.

**KATHY**
Don’t romanticize underachievement. Still community college.

A car passes the other way. Jeff drums the wheel.

**JEFF**
Saw a band at the skate park. Kids with bad posture, decent rhythm.

**KATHY**
I saw one at The Clubhouse. Covered REO Speedwagon like it was gospel. I cried.

**JEFF**
Classic rock should be quarantined. At least till the boomers die off.

**KATHY**
Careful. I might love Boston.

**JEFF**
God, I hope not.

**KATHY** (quiet)
Me too.

Silence again. Not heavy—just waiting.

**JEFF**
I keep having this dream. I buy tires. Not drive, not crash. Just…pick out tires.

**KATHY** (chuckling)
That’s the saddest dream I’ve ever heard. You need better ones.

**JEFF**
Yeah. Probably do.

They both stare ahead. US 40 stretches flat and endless.

**KATHY** (after a beat)
I don’t think we’re talking about tires.

**JEFF**
Nope.

**KATHY**
Still not ready to talk about it?

**JEFF**
Nope.

**KATHY**
Cool.

Jeff flips on the low beams though the road is empty. They keep driving.

---

## **INT. JEFF’S CAR – US 40 – DUSK**

The highway stretches flat and endless, bleeding west into a burnt-orange sky. The Escort rattles in rhythm with the seams in the asphalt. Hot air pours through the half-cracked windows, whipping strands of **KATHY**’s hair into her face.  Jeff’s hands choke the wheel at ten and two. He sneaks another glance—longer this time. Bare shoulder. Freckled skin catching light. No strap. No shirt. Just Kathy.  His gaze falters down—collarbone, the slope of her chest. Real. Present. Not posed, not imagined. Just there.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Jesus Christ. Six miles. She’s been topless for six miles. Sitting right here. Next to me. And I missed it.

He jerks his eyes back to the road, face flaming, gripping the wheel harder like it might leap from his hands.  He swallows. Tries to talk. Fails. Tries again.

**JEFF**
So…uh…what do you want to do? After Clark Tech?

**KATHY** (still gazing out the window)
Maybe transfer. Maybe Europe. Come back pierced, insufferable. Or just keep playing.

Jeff risks another glance. Her breasts are in his periphery, sweat-sheened, alive in the dying light. He looks away before it tips into staring.

**JEFF**
Music’s everything, isn’t it?

**KATHY** (smiles faintly)
It’s the only thing that doesn’t lie.

The silence shifts—less awkward, more electric. The hum of the tires fills it. Jeff loosens his shoulders, lets himself breathe.

The Golden Arches flare ahead, neon cutting the dusk. Gravel crunches under the Escort’s tires as Jeff turns in.

**JEFF**
We’re here.

Kathy finally looks at him, pushing her hair from her eyes. A crooked smile.

**KATHY**
Took you long enough.

She opens the door, barefoot, unhurried, unashamed. Jeff doesn’t gawk. He marvels.

---

## **INT. FORD ESCORT – DUSK**

**KATHY**
Thanks for the ride, Jeff.

She grabs her backpack from the floor, pulls out a balled-up band tee. The reflex is automatic—shirt back on before public eyes.

But she hesitates.

## **INT. MCDONALD’S – CONTINUOUS**

The automatic door whooshes open. The blast of ice-cold AC slaps her sweat-slick skin. The place smells like fryer oil and Pine-Sol.

She freezes.

At the counter: a man. Shirtless. Trucker tan, matted chest hair, jeans stained with grease. Laughing with the cashier, loud and easy.

Above him: a bright red sign.

> [!attention]
> <font color="#ff0000">NO SHIRT. NO SHOES. NO SERVICE.</font>

Kathy’s eyes flick from the sign, to the man’s glistening torso, down to her own bare chest.

**KATHY (V.O.)**
So that’s how it is. His nipples get a pass. Mine get parole denied.

The T-shirt in her hand goes slack. Drops to the tile with a wet slap. The sound ricochets through the restaurant.

**SOUND:** A fry hits the floor. A straw squeaks free of a lid. Someone gasps.

Kathy steps forward, topless, chin high. Each stride makes her breasts bounce, punctuation marks in a sentence she’s writing for the whole room.  Jeff slips in behind her, confused.

**JEFF**
Kathy? What are you—

He sees her. Stops dead. His mouth works, but nothing comes out.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Her nipples just declared war.

Kathy plants herself at the counter, shoulder to shoulder with the hairy trucker. He shrinks instantly, sensing he’s been upstaged.

**KATHY**
Big Mac. Large fries. Coke. And hurry. I’m freezing.

The cashier—Brenda, sixteen, visor crooked—stares like she’s watching a UFO land. She fumbles with the register, hands trembling. Doesn’t say no. Doesn’t even question it. Just rings her up.

Behind Kathy: chaos. A mother shielding her son’s eyes too late. A teenager choking on a McNugget. A man pretending to study the menu, jaw slack.

**KATHY (V.O.)**
Let them look. Let them whisper. My tits aren’t obscene. The hypocrisy is.

Jeff hangs back by the door, paralyzed. Awe. Terror. Lust. Laughter bottled in his throat. He can’t move. Can’t look away.  Kathy stands tall. Bare-chested, sweat cooling on her skin, unshakable. Her arms loose at her sides. Her breasts not weapons, not ornaments—just hers. And that’s enough to break the room in half.  The trucker mutters something about “crazy” and slinks out. The fryer keeps frying. The soda machine keeps humming. Kathy doesn’t hide behind crossed arms.  The cashier slides a tray across the counter with shaking hands.  Kathy takes it. No thanks, no smile, no shame. Just grabs her food like she owns the place.

**VISUAL:** The crumpled band tee still on the floor, soaking up grease from a dropped fry.

Kathy walks toward a booth, topless, unstoppable. Jeff follows like a man stumbling after a comet.  The room belongs to Kathy.

# **CHAPTER TWO: I AM THE PSYCHED LONE RANGER**

---

## **INT. JEFF’S BEDROOM – EARLY MORNING – OCTOBER 1990**

Thin light cuts through the blinds, striping the room like prison bars. The space is half-lived-in, half-abandoned: a tangle of sheets, dog-eared **Analog Science Fiction** mags, and a laundry basket that’s lost the war. The clock glows: **7:43 A.M..**

Jeff lies flat on his back, eyes open, ceiling staring contest. His chest rises and falls too fast for someone still in bed. Sweat beads—not heat, just tension.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
I didn’t sleep. Not really. Every time I closed my eyes, there she was. Not naked. Not topless. Just…Kathy. Calm. Defiant. Ordering a fucking Big Mac like she owned the joint.

(beat)
And me? Behind her. Cowardly. Covered. Pathetic.

He shifts under the sheets. The tent is obvious. Persistent. Annoyed. Alive.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Six weeks. Six weeks and my dick still thinks it’s that night. Every morning, the same drill.

He peels the blanket down, shoves pajama bottoms halfway off his hips. His hand hovers, then settles on his penis. Slow strokes, mechanical, like he’s checking a box.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It’s not even about sex anymore. It’s pressure. Static. My brain’s a shaken soda bottle and this is the only way to keep from exploding.

He closes his eyes.

**FLASH CUTS:**
- Kathy topless at the garage mic stand.
- Kathy’s boots squeaking on the McDonald’s tile.
- Kathy brushing past him in the dark, her hand grazing his.
- Kathy’s crooked smile in the Escort’s dying light.

His rhythm picks up.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Don’t think. Don’t imagine. Don’t hope. Just finish.

The clock ticks. Bedsprings creak. His breath grows shallow.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
She smells like cherry shampoo. Cigarettes. Sweat. That smell’s better than porn.

A grunt. Shoulders tense. A strangled exhale. He snatches a tissue, half-hearted cleanup. Cum lingers, tacky on his stomach.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
There. Reset. Neurons clear. Testosterone purged.

He collapses back, staring at the ceiling again. The silence presses in.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
And I still just want to talk to her.

A beat. He wipes his hands, tosses tissues in the trash. His eyes fall to the blank calendar on the wall—empty squares marching forward, identical.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
All I got is silence. A warm hand that doesn’t know what it wants. I can solve calculus in my head, but I can’t ask Kathy if she likes coffee.

He pulls his pajama bottoms up, face flat, resigned.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
That’s the problem. Not the hard-on. Not the cleanup. The silence.

The alarm flips to **7:46.** Another day starting. Nothing solved.

## **SMASH CUT TO: THE GARAGE – EARLY AFTERNOON**

The gear is set up. Amps hum faintly. No one plays. Dust motes drift in golden light. The whole space feels like it’s holding its breath.  **JEFF** fiddles with his bass strap, twitching fingers searching for busywork.  **KATHY** enters in black jeans and a tied band tee, cigarette tucked behind her ear. She pauses at the sight of him.

**KATHY**
You look like you just cut the wrong wire.

**JEFF** (startled)
Yeah. That sounds about right.

She moves past him, casual, but clocks his nervous energy. He stares at the floor.

**JEFF**
Kathy…can I talk to you?

She tilts her head, surprised. Sets her jacket on an amp.

**KATHY**
Alright. Talk.

**JEFF**
I know I’ve been—off. Around you. Or not around you. Both.

**KATHY** (smiling faintly)
You alphabetize vinyl for fun. Off comes with the territory.

**JEFF**
No, this is different. I’ve…tried to ignore it. To just play, pretend I don’t notice you. But I do. I notice everything. And—

She cuts him gently.

**KATHY**
Jeff. Plain English.

**JEFF** (blurts)
I like you.

(beat)
I’ve liked you for months. I didn’t say it because I was scared it would ruin this. Or you’d laugh. Or worse—you’d say yes, and I’d have no idea what to do.

Silence. Kathy studies him. Arms folded, face unreadable.

**KATHY**
Jeff…I like you too. Always have.

**JEFF** (disbelieving)
No. You…what? I thought you just tolerated me.

**KATHY**
I don’t strip down in front of people I tolerate.

(beat)
I just figured you’d catch up eventually.

Jeff laughs nervously, scratching the back of his neck.

**JEFF**
I jerked off this morning.

**KATHY** (chokes a laugh)
Jesus Christ.

**JEFF**
I mean—because I thought if I did, I could talk to you like a person, not a raccoon short-circuiting on hormones. Didn’t work. Still feel like my brain’s melting when you walk in.

Kathy shakes her head, grinning now, but soft.

**KATHY**
You could’ve just said hi. Or kissed me in the car that night instead of staring at my mouth like it was multiple choice.

**JEFF**
I wanted to.

**KATHY**
So did I.

They stand in the charged quiet. Not romantic, not clean—just honest.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
Look, I’m not here to fix you. But I’m not waiting forever either.

**JEFF**
What do you want?

She steps close—close enough that he feels her heat. Smoke and soap cling to her.

**KATHY**
I want you to stop being scared of me. I want this—whatever it is—to be in your day, not stuck in your head.

(beat)
And yeah, someday? I wanna fuck. Not because you owe me. Because I want you. Even the awkward, overthinking parts.

She taps his chest lightly. A warning, a promise.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
But only if you want it too.

She grabs her guitar, slings it on, plugs in.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
Now. Let’s play.

Jeff stands there, bass in hand, rattled but alive.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
She didn’t just walk into the room. She carved a door in me and walked through like she owned it.

He smiles—small, shaky, real.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S DRIVEWAY – MOMENTS LATER**

The battered yellow Celica staggers up the street, sputter-choke-growling like a lawn mower with delusions of grandeur. Suspension squeaks, rust flakes. It rolls half into the yard, half onto the drive, and dies in a hiccup.

**SOUND:** From the trunk—a faint *thud*.

---

## **INT. CELICA – CONTINUOUS**

**JIM** leans over the wheel, peering through a cracked windshield like it’s gaslighting him.

**JIM** (to himself)
C’mon, you bastard. Five more minutes and I’ll buy you real oil.

He twists the key off. The engine coughs twice, sighs, and quits.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

Inside, **KATHY** tunes her guitar, cigarette now tucked behind her ear. **JEFF** hovers near his amp, pretending to fuss with cables but really watching her.

From outside:

**JIM (O.S.)**
HEY! Somebody with a spine come help me!

Jeff blinks, like surfacing from underwater.

**KATHY** (grinning)
Duty calls, Romeo.

He exhales, heads out.

---

## **EXT. DRIVEWAY – CONTINUOUS**

Jim’s at the trunk, already sweating. He throws it open to reveal: a massive 24-inch kick drum, half-swaddled in cardboard, wedged between cracked cymbal stands, greasy fast-food bags, and a lone quart of Quaker State.

**JIM**
Ta-da. Pawn shop special. Three snare rims and part of my soul. Worth it.

**JEFF**
That thing’s enormous.

**JIM**
It’s not a drum. It’s artillery.

**KATHY (O.S.)**
Need backup?

She strolls over, cigarette lit now, dangling loose from her lips like she was born with it.

**JIM**
Bless you. Both of you. Gilley wouldn’t touch this unless I bribed him with beer or sexual favors. Sometimes both.

They wrestle the drum out. It’s bulky, awkward, weight uneven. Jeff ends up shouldering most of it. Jim pretends otherwise.

**JEFF**
This is gonna rattle the whole house.

**JIM**
Good. I want Mr. Gilley to feel it in his fillings.

**KATHY**
He won’t say a word. Just burn a hole through us with his eyes.

They shuffle toward the garage. The edge of the drum bounces into Jeff’s crotch. He winces.

**JIM**
Careful. That’s where the magic happens.

**JEFF** (flat)
Not the way I want.

Kathy catches it. Her eyes flick to him—brief, unreadable.

They heave the drum inside, drop it with a floor-shaking THUD.

**JIM** (stretching wide)
Alright. Let’s make noise. Let’s make God cry.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – MOMENTS LATER**

The new kick drum sits front and center, gleaming under a shaft of dust-speckled light. **JEFF** tightens the last clamp. **JIM** tweaks a cymbal stand. **KATHY** flicks her cigarette into the driveway, grabs a broom, and sweeps without ceremony.

They move like clockwork: casual, practiced, efficient. This is ritual.

**JIM** (grunting, wiping sweat)
Gilley’s old man’s a saint, Jeff.

**KATHY**
No kidding. Anyone else would’ve tossed us out after the second noise complaint.

**JEFF**
He says he doesn’t hear it.

(beat)
Which is bullshit, but I’ll take it.

**JIM**
He hears it. Just doesn’t care. Zen monk in denim.

**KATHY**
This garage? It’s gold. Bands out there pay by the hour in basements that smell like piss and mildew. We get eleven on the volume knob and leave the gear standing. All it costs us is sweeping up motor oil and beer cans.

She sweeps around the kick like it’s sacred.

**JEFF**
And reminding Bill not to lean his synth on the paint cans again.

**JIM**
Best deal in town.

They stop for a moment. The garage breathes with them—settled, not awkward.

**KATHY** (soft)
We’re lucky.

**JIM**
Yeah.

**JEFF**
Easy to forget. But yeah.

A rare three-way look: no bravado, no irony, just agreement.

**SOUND:** Outside, a lawnmower drones steady, mulching leaves. Inside, a tuning fork hums as Kathy strikes it.

**JEFF**
Let’s not screw it up.

**JIM**
Too late. But we’ll make it sound cool.

Laughter bounces off the walls—loose, honest—like the first chord of a song waiting to be played.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S DRIVEWAY – MINUTES LATER**

The faint rumble of **BILL DAVENPORT’s** Honda Prelude breaks the fall quiet. The engine coughs but struts, like it wants everyone to know Whitesnake’s still trapped in the tape deck.  He parks crooked, as always. Tight jeans. Boots. Leather jacket that screams synthwave samurai. A flight case slung over his shoulder like a holy relic.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

Golden light streaks across amps and cables. **KATHY** noodles scales. **JEFF** tightens a string. **JIM** fiddles with the kick pedal, tapping impatient little fills on the rim.  The door creaks. **BILL** strides in with his synth flight case, leather jacket trying too hard to look dangerous. He sets the case down carefully, like it’s a fragile part of himself.

**BILL**
Alright—five minutes. Band meeting.

The others exchange a glance.

**KATHY**
That sounds ominous.

**JIM**
What is this, Davenport, another car manifesto?

**BILL** (grins, but earnest)
Shut it. I’m serious.

He draws a breath, bouncing slightly on his toes.

**BILL (CONT’D)**
Seattle’s the thing right now. I get it. Flannel, feedback, all that noise. But everyone’s already lining up to sound like Nirvana’s kid cousin, and I don’t wanna be in that line.

**JEFF**
So what, you hate grunge?

**BILL**
No. I hate copying.

(beat)
I’ve been listening to our demos. And…when the synths come in? It’s different. It’s sharper. Feels like…like the song grows fangs.

**KATHY** (teasing, but intrigued)
Fangs, huh?

**BILL**
Yeah. We can do distortion like anybody else, but when I push textures—pads, arps, weird shit—it feels like us. Not Seattle. Not anyone. Just us.

He stops himself, suddenly aware he’s said more than he meant to.

**JIM**
Huh. “Depeche Mode with a chainsaw,” right?

**BILL** (half embarrassed, half proud)
Something like that.

A pause. The garage hums with the weight of what he’s asking—not a reinvention, just a shot at being original.

**KATHY** (slow smile)
Well, damn. The kid’s idealistic.

**JEFF**
Not wrong, though. Just…loud about it.

**BILL**
Loud’s better than forgettable.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
He didn’t know it yet, but this was Bill’s line in the sand. Young, naïve, arrogant—but desperate for something of his own. His synths weren’t just gear. They were the first flag he ever planted.

The others exchange looks. Something’s shifted.

**KATHY**
Alright, Davenport. You got your five minutes.

(beat)
Now plug in. Let’s see if your teeth can bite.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S BACK DOOR – SECONDS LATER**

The screen door groans open, its hinges rasping like a drunk throat clearing.

**JEREMY GILLEY** emerges barefoot, linen blazer glowing white against a hot pink tank. Acid-wash jeans cuffed lazy. A cigarette dangles sideways from his lips, somehow immune to gravity.

**SOUND:** A dog barks down the street. Gilley doesn’t give a shit.

**GILLEY**
What’s this? Band meeting, or did somebody call a caucus?

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

Heads turn. The garage air thickens, like charisma just sucked out the oxygen.

**BILL**
Speak of the devil. And he brought linen.

**KATHY**
Jesus, Gilley. You look like you misplaced a kilo off Key West.

**GILLEY**
It’s called *style.* Do try to keep up.

He flicks the cigarette into an empty beer can—dead center. Kicks the doorstop into place without breaking stride.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
My old man’s got the water heater on full blast. Feels like a steam room in there.

(beat)
Perfect weather for art.

**JIM**
Perfect weather for trench foot.

**JEFF**
Or death by dehydration.

Gilley drifts across the room, toeing the new kick drum.

**GILLEY**
Damn. You finally bought a drum that didn’t crawl out of a landfill?

**JIM**
Don’t get attached. It’s still on layaway.

**GILLEY**
Credit is punk rock, baby.

He stops center stage, casual as a crime scene. Grabs a mic stand, spins it once, owns the silence.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
So what are we this week? Grunge? Synthwave? Don’t tell me ska.

**BILL**
We’re trying not to sound like everyone else.

**GILLEY**
Then let’s stop talking and sound like *us.*

He leans into the mic like a king to his throne.

**KATHY (V.O.)**
Barefoot. Smoking. Dressed like a nightclub bouncer from a bad Magnum P.I. knockoff.

(beat)
And still—front man. Effortless. The idol I wanted to be.

(beat)
God help me, I thought: I can have it all.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S DRIVEWAY – LATE AFTERNOON**

The sun hangs low, still hot, glaring like it doesn’t want to quit. A dusty tan Dodge Aries wheezes to a stop, rattling like a smoker’s cough. The door creaks open.

**JEREMY EVANS** hauls himself out, every step a negotiation with gravity. His T-shirt, once gray, is dark with sweat. Cargo shorts sag shapeless. His buzzcut is glued to his scalp. A smear across his arm—beer, grease, who knows.

He grips his guitar case in one hand, thousand-yard stare in the other. He looks like the truck depot spit him out whole.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

**KATHY** adjusts pedals. **JEFF** checks tuning. **BILL** scowls at his synth cables. **JIM** tests his kick with lazy taps.

The door groans open. Jeremy staggers in.

**JIM**
Evans. Christ. You look like regret grew legs.

**JEREMY** (flat, gravel)
Started loading beer trucks for my dad at four A.M.

He sets his guitar case down—careful, reverent. Like it’s glass. Unlike himself.

**JEREMY (CONT’D)**
Warehouse was a hundred and ten. Trucks hotter. Kegs sweating. Slippery as hell.

(beat)
Almost killed myself with a case of Miller High Life.

**BILL**
Jesus.

**KATHY**
That’s a Robocop death. Ohio cut.

**JEFF**
You good?

**JEREMY**
Yeah. Just…not.

(beat)
But I’m here. Let’s make noise before I forget how to feel things.

The air shifts. They all see it: Jeremy’s spent, eyes bloodshot, hands raw. But he came anyway.

**KATHY** (quiet, sincere)
Respect.

**JIM**
Goddamn soldier.

**BILL**
And I was about to complain about my cables.

Jeremy exhales—somewhere between a laugh and a groan. He plugs in. No warm-up. No chatter. Just a long, ragged power chord.

It hangs there. Heavy. Perfect.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
Didn’t know if my fingers would move. Didn’t care. Music was the only thing left that didn’t ask for a punch card.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – LATE AFTERNOON**

Amps hum low, cords snake across the floor. Everyone’s in pre-set ritual: knobs turned, straps tugged, mics adjusted.

**GILLEY** — shirtless, sweat-sheened, smug by default — drifts toward **KATHY**. She’s crouched over her battered distortion pedal, tweaking knobs like she’s punishing them.

He leans in just close enough to invade her space, hovering like a kiss that never lands.

**GILLEY**
If you keep glaring at that pedal, it’s gonna straighten up out of fear.

**KATHY** (still focused)
If only that worked on people.

**GILLEY**
You talking about me?

The corner of her mouth betrays her — a twitch, not a smile, not quite.

**KATHY**
If the boot fits.

**GILLEY**
Boot fits fine. Question is, you wanna try walking in ’em again?

She looks up now, eyes steady.

**KATHY**
That was a long walk. And I remember the blisters.

A pause — longer than it should be.

**KATHY (CONT’D)**
I’m not in the market for reruns.

**GILLEY** (shrug, cocky)
Not a rerun. Remastered edition.

**KATHY**
Dolby surround and everything?

**GILLEY**
Only the best, sis.

She laughs — short, involuntary, unguarded.

**KATHY**
I’ve got riffs to write, Gilley. Not time to babysit your libido.

She bends to her amp, hair falling forward to mask the smile still tugging at her mouth. He sees it. He plants the moment like a flag, strolling off casual.

---

## **ACROSS THE ROOM**

**JEFF** watches from behind his bass. Pretends to tweak a tone knob that’s fine. Too fine. His face is blank — the kind of blank that creaks under pressure. Jaw tightens once.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It was nothing. Old sparks. Just Gilley being Gilley.

(beat)
But she smiled. Not at me. Not like that.

His eyes flick up. Kathy catches it, just for a second, then looks away too quickly.

The tension hardens — silent, invisible, deafening.

**JIM**
Can we play before everybody starts fucking again?

**BILL**
Don’t tempt me.

Laughter breaks out — loose, nervous. A crack in the air. But the moment doesn’t vanish. It lingers, a live wire in the room.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – EARLY EVENING**

Break time. The garage reeks of sweat, dust, and stale amp heat.   **KATHY** and **JIM** are outside smoking.  **GILLEY** is off in a corner, ranting about setlists to nobody who asked.

**JEFF** sits on a milk crate in the back, bass on his lap, plucking the same two strings like a stress toy. His eyes are glued to the concrete floor, like it might give him an answer.

**BILL** wanders over, wipes his palms on a bandana, and drops down across from him without invitation.

**BILL**
You always do this.

**JEFF**
Do what?

**BILL**
The brooding. The “I’m the moody bass player in an afterschool special” routine.

(beat)
You messed up in the head, or what?

Jeff looks up, startled.

**JEFF**
The hell, Bill?

**BILL**
I’m serious. It’s like you’re missing a card in the deck. Like when they were handing out the “connect to people” chip, you were already in line for spare parts.

(leans in)
Kathy wants to fuck you, man.

Jeff’s fingers freeze. He doesn’t breathe.

**BILL (CONT’D)**
And she’s not even subtle. Which is wild, because Kathy doesn’t send out that vibe for free. She’s aiming it right at you. I know it. You know it. Gilley knows it. Probably Mr. Gilley knows it.

**JEFF** (quiet, panicked)
Jesus—keep your voice down.

**BILL**
Why? Afraid she’ll hear the truth?

He smirks, then shifts gears, a little sharper now.

**BILL (CONT’D)**
Look, you’re smart. Fine. You live in your head. Fine. But you keep acting like you need to solve this mathematically before you move.  She’s just waiting for you to stop acting like you’re allergic to your own dick.

**JEFF**
It’s not that simple.

**BILL**
It is. You’re scared. And instead of doing something, you’re hiding in the corner, stewing. That’s how you lose her.

**JEFF**
I don’t want to screw it up.

**BILL**
You’re screwing it up by standing still.

(beat, softer)
What are you waiting for, man? A written invitation?

Jeff stares at the floor. His jaw works, tight. He doesn’t answer.  Bill watches him for a beat, then exhales, shaking his head.

**BILL**
You’re not broken, Jeff. You just act like you want to be.

He stands, dusts off his hands, and leaves Jeff in the corner with his silence.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It was easier when people thought I was just weird. At least that gave me cover.

(beat)
But he wasn’t wrong.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

**JEFF** is still hunched on the crate, eyes on the floor. Bill’s words linger in the air like smoke that won’t clear.

**BILL** takes two steps away, shaking his head—

**JEREMY EVANS (O.S.)**
The hell did you just say to my brother?

The garage freezes.

**JEREMY** fills the doorway, arms crossed, guitar strap hanging loose over his shoulder, shirt soaked through. His face is red—not from the workday. From fury.

**BILL** (defensive, palms out)
I was just saying what everyone’s thinking.

**JEREMY** (without remorse)
Don’t.

He steps forward, voice low, flat. A blade dragged slow across stone.

**JEREMY (CONT’D)**
Don’t ever come at Jeff like that again.

**BILL**
Come on, man. I’m not starting anything. I’m saying he can’t sit there forever. He’s gotta—

**JEREMY** (cutting him off)
I said *don’t.*

Another step. He’s between them now, nose to nose with Bill.

**JEREMY (CONT’D)**
You don’t get to call him broken. He isn’t. He’s my brother. He feels everything—he just doesn’t put it on display for assholes to measure.

Bill stiffens, caught off guard.

**BILL**
I never said—

**JEREMY** (voice climbing)
You meant it. Don’t bullshit me.

(beat)
He’s the smartest person I know. He’s got more heart than half this county. So you will respect him.

(steps closer, eyes locked)
Or I’ll put you through that fuckin’ garage door.

**SILENCE.**

Even the amps seem to hum lower. Bill doesn’t move—posture taut, stunned, but not backing down.

Then—

**JEFF**
Jer.

Jeremy glances back. Jeff’s still on the crate, face flushed, somewhere between mortified and moved.

**JEFF (CONT’D)**
It’s okay. He’s…not all the way wrong.

**JEREMY**
He’s still outta line.

**JEFF**
Maybe. But not wrong.

The words hang. Bill exhales, nods once—conceding just enough.

**BILL**
Fair.

(beat, to Jeff)
Just don’t forget you’ve got people who give a shit.

He turns, retreats to his synth—the safe place, the noise.  Jeremy stays planted, watching him go. Then back to Jeff, softer now.

**JEREMY**
Don’t let him crawl inside your head. You’re not broken. You’re you.

He gives Jeff’s shoulder a solid pat, then moves to tune his guitar, like nothing happened.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
I wanted to say something. Anything. But the words jammed up like bad frets.

(beat)
So I picked up my bass. Let it talk for me.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S DRIVEWAY – LATE EVENING**

The sun’s almost gone. Heat lingers, sticky and stubborn.

**JEFF** stands at the back of his dented Ford Escort, hatch up. He digs past cables, receipts, and an old denim jacket until his hand lands on a CD — shrink-wrap still on.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
She gave it to me a week ago. Said, “You’ll like it. They’re from Cincy. Doing something different.”

(beat)
I listened once. Hid it under the junk. Like I wasn’t ready.

He holds the CD up. **Up In It – The Afghan Whigs.**  This time, he doesn’t put it back.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – MOMENTS LATER**

Amps hum idle. **KATHY** perches on an amp with a Coke. **JIM** taps lazy rhythms on his sticks. **BILL** is bent over his synth, twisting knobs like he’s interrogating them.

**JEFF** approaches, silent, then extends the CD.

**JEFF**
Thought you might wanna hear this.

**BILL** (skeptical)
What is it?

**JEFF**
*Up In It*. Afghan Whigs. Cincy guys. Soul cut with post-punk. Not Seattle. Not synth-pop. Something in between.

**BILL** takes it, turns it over in his hands.

**BILL**
You finally listened?

**JEFF**
Today. After…everything.

(beat)
Figured it was worth sharing.

**BILL**
So this is you burying the hatchet?

**JEFF**
This is me handing you something I didn’t write.

(beat)
See what we can steal. Or avoid. Either way, us, not clones

**BILL** studies him. The fight’s still in the air, but thinner now. He tears the wrap with his thumbnail.

**BILL**
You giving me this ‘cause it’s music, or ‘cause your brother nearly tore my head off?

**JEFF**
Bit of both.

**KATHY** (still sipping Coke)
Told you it had teeth.

Bill crosses, slides the disc into a beat-to-hell boom box.

**SOUND:** “*Retarded*.” Dark. Soulful. A groove that smolders.

The garage stills. Even **GILLEY**, mid-rant in the corner, stops muttering and listens.

**KATHY** (quiet, satisfied)
See? Different.

**BILL**
Yeah. Not grunge. Not shiny. Something else.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It wasn’t just a record. It was a bridge. From her to me. From me to Bill. From this garage to somewhere bigger than Bethel Township.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

SOUND: The last soft, echoing groove of “*Retarded*” fades out, leaving nothing but the hum of amps and the stunned silence of five people trying to process what the hell they just heard.

No one moves.

**JIM** stands near the kit, mouth slightly open, like he’s staring at a ghost that told him a secret.

He slowly sets his drumsticks down on the snare like they’re holy relics.

**JIM**
Holy shit.

(beat)
That wasn’t music.

(beat)
That was the voice of Ohio.

SILENCE. Nobody laughs. Nobody rolls their eyes. Because they feel it, too.

**BILL**
Okay, yeah. That was serious.

**KATHY**
Welcome to what I’ve been trying to tell you for a week.

**JEFF** looks at her — not directly, but enough. She doesn’t look back, just smiles into her Coke can like she knew.  Then, from the corner, wiping sweat from his brow with the hem of his shirt, **JEREMY** Evans speaks.

**JEREMY EVANS** (plain, no hesitation)
I’d take **JEREMY** **GILLEY** five times a week and twice on Sunday if he ever sang like that.

Everyone turns.

**GILLEY**, who’s been stretching his shoulders and humming some off-key half-song, freezes mid-movement.

**GILLEY**
Wait, what?

**JIM** (choking on laughter)
Oh my God—

**BILL**
Get in line, bro. He’s got backup singers now.

**KATHY**
Gilley, that voice you just heard? That’s what you think you sound like when you’ve had three beers and start quoting Bowie.

**GILLEY**
I do sound like that after three beers.

**JEREMY EVANS**
You sound like a guy choking on a harmonica.

Laughter explodes in the room, loud and unfiltered. The ice is broken. The spell has lifted, but the effect lingers.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
For once, nobody was talking about the future. Or the past. Or who liked who.

(beat)
We were just five idiots in a garage, hearing something we weren’t sure we deserved.

---

# **CHAPTER THREE: THE SUMMER THAT LASTED FOREVER**

---

## **INT. BEER WAREHOUSE – DOWNTOWN DAYTON WASTELANDS – NOON**

The place is a furnace. Dust drifts in shafts of sunlight like ash. The concrete floor is slick from sweating kegs.

**JEREMY EVANS** hoists another keg onto a pallet, back straining, shirt plastered to him like wet paper. His hands are raw, but the rhythm of the work is practiced—pivot the keg, hook under the rim, lift with the thighs, set it just so. No wasted motion.

At the far end, **MR. EVANS**—fifties, beer gut earned honest—leans against a clipboard and a forklift that hasn’t worked right since Reagan. His eyes track just enough to make sure Jeremy doesn’t drop dead.

**MR. EVANS**
You scrub the men’s bathroom yet?

**JEREMY**
Not yet.

(grunting as he sets another keg)
Why? Something explode in there?

**MR. EVANS**
Might’ve. Or somebody died. Hard to say. But if you get in there after this shift—knees on tile, bleach in hand—I might scrounge something up for you.

Jeremy pauses, arm draped over a keg. He knows this rhythm too. Dad doesn’t bargain unless there’s bait.

**JEREMY**
What kind of “something”?

**MR. EVANS**
Tickets.

(beat)
Four of ‘em. Bogart’s. Week and a half from now.

Jeremy straightens, sweat dripping. He blinks.

**JEREMY**
Who’s playing?

His dad squints at scribbles on the clipboard.

**MR. EVANS**
That band your brother won’t shut up about. Afghanistan…Whigs?

**JEREMY**
The Afghan Whigs.

**MR. EVANS**
Yeah, them. Sound like they gargle with gravel and heartbreak. Real cheerful.

Jeremy’s eyes light up like he just hit the lottery.

**JEREMY**
You’re serious?

**MR. EVANS**
I don’t joke about free tickets, boy. Especially not ones I could pawn for breaker money.

He tears a note from the clipboard and holds it up like scripture.

**MR. EVANS (CONT’D)**
You want ’em? Make that bathroom sparkle like the Vatican on Easter.

Jeremy doesn’t hesitate. He salutes with a rag off the shelf.

**JEREMY**
Yes, sir. General.

**MR. EVANS**
And use bleach. Don’t just light a match and pray.

---

## **INT. MEN’S BATHROOM – LATER**

The door creaks open. Jeremy steps into a tiled hell. The stink hits first: ammonia, piss, sweat. Forty truckers a day, and it shows.  He pulls his T-shirt collar over his nose, cracks his knuckles, and surveys the battlefield. Stalls tagged with Sharpie. Urinals ringed with calcified yellow. A sink clogged with paper towels.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
It wasn’t music. It wasn’t romance. It was survival.

(beat)
My old man always said: you don’t half-ass a shit job. You whole-ass it.

He ties the rag tight around his hand, grabs the bleach, and goes to work. Efficient. Surgical.  Toilet first—scrub hard, circles tight, lid slamming down like punctuation. Stall walls next—graffiti fades under elbow grease. Floor tiles scrubbed until the grout lightens a shade. He doesn’t gag. Doesn’t blanch. Just works.

Sweat drips into the bleach water. His spine protests, but his arms don’t stop.

**SOUND:** Rag slapping tile, brush grinding porcelain, the steady rhythm of someone turning filth into order.

Jeremy wrings the mop once, hard, then angles it just right under the last urinal to catch the run-off.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
Clean enough to eat off. Not that I’d recommend it.

He leans back, panting, mop dripping, and looks at the mirror. His reflection’s a mess—hair plastered, shirt ruined, eyes red. But behind the exhaustion there’s a glint: pride. Not joy, not glory. Pride.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
Four tickets. Enough to hand Jeff one without a word. Enough to make all this worth it.

---

## **INT. MEN’S BATHROOM – BEER WAREHOUSE – LATE AFTERNOON**

The buzzing fluorescent light overhead flickers like it’s giving up on life.
