**JEREMY EVANS** crouches beside a rusted toilet, knuckles white on a brush that’s down to its last bristles. A jug of industrial-grade bleach sits open next to him, the fumes sharp enough to burn through regret. His knees ache. His shirt sticks to his back. His soul feels one flush away from detachment.

**SOUND:** Harsh slosh-scrub. Water dripping from a sink that hasn’t worked right since the Bicentennial.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
This is the price I pay.

(beat)
Four tickets to Bogart’s. Sixty bucks worth of sweaty joy and soul-grit. In exchange for scrubbing forty truckers’ wreckage out of tile grout.

He leans back, shirt pulled over his nose, blinking away bleach sting.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
But it hit me, right here in the ammonia haze:

(beat)
There’s six of us. Six people. Four tickets.

He glances at the cracked urinal, the graffiti-scarred mirror, the soap crusted like amber in the dispenser.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
I’m no math whiz, but that dog don’t hunt.

Jeremy stands, knees popping, grabs a fresh rag. He doesn’t slow down. He *accelerates*. Wipes the sinks in tight circles. Re-scrubs the urinals, edge to edge. Digs his fingernails into grout with a vengeance. He’s not just cleaning; he’s attacking entropy itself.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
Dad always said: you don’t half-ass a shit job. You whole-ass it.

He flips the “Out of Order” sign off the stall, tosses it into the trash, then kneels to scrape gum off the underside of the toilet seat with a flathead screwdriver. His face is grim, efficient, relentless.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
Because that’s how you buy another day.

(beat)
Make it clean. Make it right. Earn the ticket. Live to play another riff tomorrow.

He flushes, watches the swirl pull away the day’s labor. He stands, wipes his forearm across his brow, and stares at the mirror. His reflection is a ruin — shirt soaked, eyes red, hair matted. But behind the fatigue, there’s the stubborn glint of survival.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
Someone’s gonna be left out. Or someone’s gonna step up.

(beat)
So I did what any emotionally avoidant little brother would do.

He picks up the bleach bottle, caps it tight, and grins despite himself.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
I decided to pester the shit out of my dad.

---

## **INT. LOADING BAY – LATE AFTERNOON**

The air smells like diesel and yeast. A truck door slams outside. The buzz of a forklift that may or may not work drifts in from the yard.

**MR. EVANS** is hunched over a clipboard, Sharpie tucked behind one ear, lips moving as he checks off inventory.

**JEREMY** walks up, still damp from Toilet Duty Apocalypse, rag hanging from his back pocket like a flag of surrender.

**JEREMY**
Hey, Dad?

**MR. EVANS** (without looking up)
If you’re asking for gas money, the answer’s no.

**JEREMY**
Can you get two more tickets?

(beat)
Same show. Afghan Whigs. You said four, but there’s six of us.

Mr. Evans looks up slow, squinting like Jeremy just asked for a pony.

**MR. EVANS**
Christ. What is this, a field trip?

**JEREMY**
More like group therapy. But loud.

(beat)
C’mon. If we’re gonna do it, we should all do it.

Mr. Evans grunts, scratches his chin with the Sharpie.

**MR. EVANS**
What am I getting out of it?

Jeremy blinks.

**JEREMY**
What?

**MR. EVANS**
The tickets. Two more. That’s a debt. You think Cincinnati reps just hand ‘em out like Halloween candy?

He flips the clipboard around, jabs a finger at a scribble in the margins.

**MR. EVANS (CONT’D)**
I traded for the first four. You know what I had to give up? Zima.

**JEREMY** (snorts)
Zima?

**MR. EVANS**
Zima. Clear malt swill. I put my name on a case of it, and the rep walked away smiling like he’d robbed me blind.

(beat, grim)
That’s a reputation killer. I’ve spent three decades making sure no one thinks I traffic in fluorescent Kool-Aid booze. Then some desperate distributor waves Whigs tickets, and I fold. That’s the game.

Jeremy leans against a pallet, arms crossed.

**JEREMY**
So…what? You want me to bribe you back?

**MR. EVANS**
I want you to understand. This is barter. Gray market. You don’t just ask for more, you offer more.

He caps the Sharpie with a snap, points it like a gavel.

**MR. EVANS (CONT’D)**
Dayton Air Show’s coming up.

Jeremy groans. Full-body groan.

**JEREMY**
Dad. No.

**MR. EVANS**
Yes. You want six tickets? You give me a crew. Flight line chalets. VIPs. Generals who can drink a keg dry before the Thunderbirds finish their flyover.

(beat)
You’re hauling. You’re icing. You’re smiling at colonels who don’t smile back.

**JEREMY** (sighs)
So this is a work detail. A ticket-for-keg swap.

**MR. EVANS**
It’s called quid pro quo. Latin. Means stop whining and find a buddy who can lift with his legs.

Jeremy stares at him. Wipes his hands on his jeans.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
This wasn’t charity. It wasn’t even parenting.

(beat)
It was the first lesson in the gray market: nothing’s free. Everything’s a trade. You scrub a bathroom, you get four tickets. You haul beer for colonels, you get six.

(beat)
And if you want more than that? You better learn what you’ve got that someone else needs.

He smirks despite himself, shakes his head.

**JEREMY (V.O.) (CONT’D)**
Guess I just bartered my summer for two more seats at Bogart’s.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – LATE AFTERNOON**

The amps hum lazily, a restless kind of quiet. **KATHY** noodles scales. **GILLEY** lies on his back, smoking through the breeze of a box fan. **JIM** taps rimshots like Morse code for boredom. **BILL** tweaks a filter until it howls.

**JEFF** sits on a milk crate, head tilted, tuning his bass one careful turn at a time. He pauses between plucks a little longer than most, listening harder.

The garage door slams open.

**JEREMY EVANS** barrels in, sweat-streaked and triumphant, waving a folded paper like a prizefighter’s belt.

**JEREMY**
Four tickets. Afghan Whigs. Bogart’s. Week and a half.

**KATHY**
Fuck yes.

**GILLEY**
That’s culture.

**BILL**
Finally.

**JIM**
Do they serve bourbon or just watery beer?

**JEREMY** raises a finger.

**JEREMY**
Catch: it’s only four. For now. Dad’s trying to wrangle two more if I can get a crew for the Dayton Air Show. Keg-hauling, sunstroke, screaming Thunderbirds overhead. Who’s in?

**JIM**
Jesus. Did that once. Felt like a mule in a heatwave.

**GILLEY**
I blistered through sneakers last time.

**KATHY**
But tickets are tickets.

**JEREMY** looks at his brother.

**JEREMY (CONT’D)**
Jeff?

**JEFF** doesn’t answer right away. He keeps tuning — pluck, pause, adjust — like he’s buying time. Finally:

**JEFF**
Can’t. I’ve got surgery next week.

The garage stalls.

**JIM**
Wait—what?

**JEFF**
Tympanoplasty.

(beat)
They’re fixing my eardrum.

**KATHY** (startled)
Since when?

**JEFF** (shrugs)
Since always. Just…finally getting it done.

He plucks one more note, faint smile tugging.

**JEFF (CONT’D)**
General anesthesia. Scalpel. Laser. Very punk rock.

The room holds its breath.

**BILL**
That’s…kinda metal. In a medical-journal way.

**GILLEY**
So what do they do, like…swap it out with tweezers?

**JEFF** (dry)
Something like that.

**JEREMY** watches him, no joke this time, just steady. Then turns back to the group.

**JEREMY**
Alright. Jeff’s out. That leaves the rest of us. We’ll sweat it out for tickets.

**KATHY**
Fine. I’ll carry kegs if it means all six of us get in.

**JIM**
Guess we’re working the Air Show.

**GILLEY**
Hydrate or die.

**JEFF** tunes the last string, testing the note. It’s just slightly sharp. He twists again, listening close.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
One ticket costs sweat. Another costs hearing. Somehow, that felt fair.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – CONTINUOUS**

The air is thick with amps humming and hormones firing. What started as Jeremy’s pitch has turned into a constitutional crisis over four little tickets.

**KATHY**
Okay, ground rules. Nobody gets to play martyr. This isn’t church. Who’s willing to sweat for beer tickets?

**JIM**
I already did my tour of duty. Twice. I should have veteran’s benefits by now.

**GILLEY**
And I did the one where it hit 103. Sun poisoning, folding chair to the skull. That’s combat pay.

**BILL**
I’ve *never* gone. Which means I should be automatically immune. It’s called precedent. Look it up.

**KATHY**
You’re not endangered, you’re expendable.

**JEREMY**
Listen—my dad doesn’t care about precedent, or trauma, or your fragile egos. He cares if you can lift a keg without crying.

**JEFF** (barely looking up from his bass)
Should we just draw straws?

**JIM**
We’d need straws for that. And someone sober enough not to eat theirs.

**GILLEY**
Alright, let’s stop pretending. Who *deserves* to go?

**BILL**
Me. I’ve been the one shouting about originality. Half the reason we’re even talking about “sound” is me.

**KATHY**
Yeah, but I gave Jeff the Whigs CD. I lit this whole fuse.

**JEREMY**
And I got the damn tickets. You’re welcome.

**GILLEY**
I’m the front man. Face of the band. If someone’s getting inspiration from a concert, it should be me.

**KATHY**
Face of the band? More like feet of the band. They’re always up on the furniture.

**JIM**
We could sell a kidney. That’d buy two more tickets and matching T-shirts.

**JEFF**
We’re too broke for organ trafficking.

**BILL** (snapping his fingers)
Then it’s settled the way it’s always settled—trial by fire.

**KATHY**
You mean—

**BILL**
Battle of the Bands. Air Show Edition. Whoever survives gets the ticket.

**JEREMY**
That’s idiotic.

**GILLEY** (grinning)
No, no, hold on. I like where this is going.

**KATHY**
Absolutely not. We’re not turning sweat labor into Mortal Kombat.

**JIM**
We are broke, though. Like, I’m measuring gas in quarters-broke.

The group falls into a ragged silence, staring at each other like revolutionaries who just realized none of them can do math.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It wasn’t about the tickets. Not really. It was about who’d pay the price to keep this thing alive.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – MINUTES LATER**

The amps still hum, but the air’s shifted. The ticket debate has devolved into whining, excuses, and half-serious threats of violence. Nobody’s in charge. Nobody wants to lose.

Until **GILLEY** climbs onto an overturned milk crate like he was born with a spotlight. Shirtless, sweating, cigarette dangling from his lip — and somehow looking like he planned it.

He twirls a drumstick in his hand like it’s a baton.

**GILLEY**
Alright, children of the corn. Hear me and despair. We’re not drawing straws. We’re not selling kidneys. We’re not crying about kegs in the sun.

(beat, grins)
We’re competing.

**BILL**
Oh, Jesus Christ.

**KATHY**
Why do we keep letting him talk?

**JIM**
Because nobody else can make this sound like the Olympics.

**JEFF** (flat, still tuning)
I’m exempt.

**GILLEY**
Correct. Miracle Ear here gets a pass. But the rest of you? Welcome to the thunder dome.

**JEREMY** (squinting)
Please tell me this doesn’t involve running.

**GILLEY**
No running. This is about brains, brawn, and questionable decision-making. My three favorite things.

He hops off the crate, starts shoving amps and cables around to create “lanes” like a kid building an obstacle course.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
Event One: Speed Coil Wrapping. Fastest to wrap three cables without knots wins glory.

**JIM**
This is rigged. I’ve got drummer hands.

**KATHY**
So, useless flippers.

**BILL**
If I lose, I’m wrapping a coil around your neck.

**GILLEY** (laughing, delighted)
Perfect. That’s the spirit.

He sets a mic stand upright, balances the drumstick across it like it’s Excalibur.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
You want Bogart’s tickets? You want to sweat for something other than practice? Then you step into Gilley’s Gauntlet of Honor and Suffering.

Nobody moves. Then — grudging smiles start to creep in.

**KATHY** (to herself)
God help me, this idiot actually made it fun.

**JEREMY** leans toward **JEFF**, who’s still parked on his amp, arms folded.

**JEREMY**
You realize he just conned us into letting him run the show, right?

**JEFF**
Yeah.

(beat)
But somehow, it feels…right.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
You don’t always lead a band. Sometimes you just survive it. And Gilley? He was survival with a smile.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – MOMENTS LATER**

The garage is now a coliseum of stupidity. Amps shoved to the walls, pizza box scoreboard nailed to a broomstick, and **GILLEY** shirtless on a crate, ruling like a barefoot Caesar with a busted mic stand as his scepter.

**GILLEY**
Let the games begin.

---

**EVENT ONE: CABLE WRAP RACE**

Chaos. Cords fly. **BILL** coils like a surgeon, **KATHY** strangles hers into knots, **JIM** falls headfirst into a cymbal stand, and **JEREMY** cheats blatantly.

**GILLEY**
POINTS FOR STYLE. POINTS FOR BLOOD.

---

**EVENT TWO: GUITAR STRAP LIMBO**

They grunt, crawl, and contort under a dangling amp cord. **KATHY** slithers through smooth. **JIM** belly-flops. **BILL** knocks the stand over. **JEREMY** walks around it.

**GILLEY**
DISQUALIFIED FOR COWARDICE!

---

**EVENT THREE: SOLO-OFF**

Noise. Pure noise. **KATHY** seduces her strings, **BILL** makes the synth fart, **JIM** plays a pizza box, **JEREMY** crashes into “Smoke on the Water.”

**GILLEY**
OHIO CRUMBLE, BABY!

---

**EVENT FOUR: COOL-OFF STAREDOWN**

A silent circle. Sweat pours. **JIM** cracks first with armpit farts. **KATHY** loses it laughing. **BILL** folds next. It comes down to **JEREMY** and **GILLEY**, eyes locked, foreheads beading.

**JEFF** (O.S., calm)
Jeremy wins.

Everyone whips their heads. **JEFF** hasn’t moved from his amp, Coke can sweating beside him, face unreadable.

**JEFF (CONT’D)**
For dignity. And creative cheating.

**GILLEY** (shrugs, raises the mic stand)
The winners: Kathy. Jeremy. And Jim.

**JIM**
YES! I BLED FOR TICKETS!

**BILL**
Rigged.

**KATHY**
You farted on a synth. Sit down.

Laughter bursts through the garage, bouncing off the walls like a real show.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It was chaos. Dumb as hell. Perfect.

(beat)
I couldn’t hear half of it.

(beat)
But I could feel it.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – EVENING, SWEAT-SLICK & UNHINGED**

The Sharpie scoreboard on the pizza box is a mess of half-marks and doodles. The bulb overhead buzzes like it’s trying to escape. Everyone’s drenched, jittery, drunk on exhaustion.

---

**EVENT FIVE: THE “STAGE DIVE” SIMULATION**

A sad heap of three couch cushions lies on the oil-stained floor.

**GILLEY** (grandiose)
Trust fall into glory. Style earns points. Crying for your mother loses all of them.

**JIM** staggers up, salutes like he’s storming Normandy, yells *“GERONIMO!”* and launches. The cushions skitter. He lands half-on, half-off, groans like his spine folded wrong—then throws up a shaky thumbs-up.

**GILLEY**
Pain?

**JIM**
Yep.

**GILLEY**
Points.

**KATHY** steps, breathes, and dives. Swan-like. Sticks the landing. Not a hair out of place.

**GILLEY**
Awarding grace points, docking arrogance points. Net zero.

**KATHY**
Your system is a dumber than the Fresh Prince.

**GILLEY**
And yet—it rules over you.

**BILL** tiptoes forward, attempts a cautious backward sit-down.

**EVERYONE**
BOOOOO.

**GILLEY**
Negative infinity. Sit-diving is cowardice.

**JEREMY** backs up ten feet, runs, spins mid-air, shouts *“FOR JEFF!”* and misses completely—smashes shoulder-first into the garage wall.

**JEREMY** (flat on the floor)
I regret nothing.

**JEFF** (deadpan)
That was spectacular failure.

---

**EVENT SIX: THE LYRICS TRIAL**

**GILLEY** (relishing it)
Karaoke of Death. One verse. Whigs lyrics. Eye contact with me the whole time.

**BILL**
I’m already dying.

**KATHY** doesn’t hesitate. Locks eyes with Gilley and spits out a verse from *Wouldn’t That Be Something*, raw and flawless, like a challenge.

**GILLEY** (caught off guard)
Jesus Christ.

**JIM** starts confidently, then slips into Scorpions halfway through.

**GILLEY**
No. Wrong universe. Blasphemy. Points deducted for Germany.

**JEREMY** goes off-book, turns the verse into a sweaty slam-poetry sermon, arms slicing the air.

**GILLEY**
No clue what that was. Points anyway.

The last spotlight falls on **BILL**, rigid as a flagpole.

**BILL**
Fine. I’m doing Van Halen. *Drop Dead Legs.* Don’t stop me.

He stares into the middle distance and chants it flat, nasal, like a bad séance.

**BILL** (droning)
*Drop dead legs, pretty smile / Hurts my head, gets me wild…*

**JIM** (horrified)
Oh my God, make it stop.

**KATHY** (laughing, uneasy)
He’s turned it into psychological warfare.

**BILL**
*Nice white teeth, Betty Boop…*

**GILLEY** (cutting him off)
ENOUGH. That was like Leonard Cohen with mono.

**BILL** (proud)
Art should wound.

**GILLEY**
It did. All of us.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S GARAGE – NIGHT SWEAT, PIZZA BOX SCOREBOARD, CHAOS**

The Sharpie scoreboard is smeared beyond recognition. Empty Coke cans rattle in the corner. Everyone looks wrung out — but **GILLEY** is glowing, mad with power.

---

**EVENT SEVEN: THE CHUG OF REGRET**

**GILLEY** (raising a sticky Big Gulp cup like Excalibur)
The sacred vessel. Garage-warm root beer. Flat. Partly fermented. Found in my trunk under jumper cables.

(beat)
One of you must drink. The Champion will emerge. The rest will pray for death.

**JIM** snatches it without hesitation, takes two gulps. His throat makes a sound like a clogged drain. He retches, wipes his mouth.

**JIM**
Tastes like Satan’s cough syrup.

He staggers, triumphant, hands it to **KATHY**.

**KATHY** (grim)
This is for art.

She sips, instantly doubles over coughing, hurls profanity at the cup, and shoves it away.

**BILL**
No chance. I’ve smelled your trunk before.

**JEREMY** shrugs, takes it, drains the whole thing in one go. His eyes water. His face reddens.

**JEREMY**
That was…the worst thing I’ve ever loved.

**GILLEY**
Points for bravery. Points for death wish. Points for…whatever that was.

---

**EVENT EIGHT: INSTRUMENTAL CHARADES**

**GILLEY** (swaggering)
Final test. Play an emotion. No words. No excuses. We guess.

**JIM** slams out a chaotic drum blast, stops, breathless.

**KATHY**
That was “bathroom emergency.”

**BILL**
No — “my dad hugged me once and I still don’t believe it.”

**JIM**
It was envy.

**GILLEY**
Minus points. Too literal.

**JEREMY** plugs in, fuzz cranked, lets out a riff so filthy the garage shakes. It sounds like VHS sex dubbed four times over.

**JEFF** (deadpan)
…Lust.

**JEREMY**
Wrong. Guilt.

**KATHY**
Jesus Christ.

**BILL** takes his turn on synth — layers of reverb, choir patches, and a single haunting arpeggio that builds, builds, then collapses into silence.

Nobody speaks for a beat.

**JEFF** (quiet)
That was loneliness.

**BILL**
Correct.

**GILLEY** (shaking his head)
I hate this game.

---

**FINAL EVENT: THE ULTIMATE SHAME WALK**

**GILLEY** (banging a drumstick against the wall)
Final round! The crown jewel of idiocy! Thirty seconds. Give us your Grammy acceptance speech for an award you absolutely did not deserve.

**KATHY**
Oh, this is gonna smell.

**JIM** (already mid-sprint to the “stage”)
“I wanna thank my third-grade teacher for saying I’d never amount to anything — MISS BURNS, EAT IT. Also the Domino’s guy who kept us alive for three years, and God, who gave me ankles that can sorta keep time. We made it!”

He actually tears up. Nobody claps.

**KATHY** (aside)
Christ, he’s leaking.

**JEREMY** (slouching up, voice gravel)
“I’d like to thank caffeine, regret, and my brother Jeff, who once gave me half a Pop-Tart during gym. This one’s for the Evans bloodline — sturdy enough to haul kegs, dumb enough to start bands.”

**BILL**
That’s not a speech, that’s a family obituary.

**BILL** (straightening, dead serious)
“I thank myself. For being the smartest bastard in every room. Especially this garage. I played 128 MIDI patches in one night, and no one believed in me but me. You’re welcome.”

**GILLEY**
Nope. Too real. Disqualified for sincerity.

**KATHY** (steps forward, monotone)
“I’d like to thank the ghost of Joan Jett, even though she’s alive. Jeff’s sock drawer, for sheltering the Whigs CD for six days. And finally this garage, for smelling like ass, ruin, and destiny. Thank you.”

Silence. Then **JIM** slow-claps like a seal being clubbed.

**GILLEY** (awed, grossed out)
You win. Holy hell. You win.

**KATHY** (blinking)
…What did I just say?

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It was disgusting. It was stupid. It was perfect.

(beat)
And, yeah — it decided nothing about the tickets.

---

**AFTERMATH: THE RANKINGS – CONTINUOUS**

The pizza box scoreboard sits on the workbench like a holy relic of stupidity. Everyone is still glistening, out of breath, and sticky with root beer and bad decisions.

**JEREMY** slumps on a cushion like a war veteran.  **KATHY** leans against her amp, still grinning like she can’t believe half the words that came out of her mouth.  **JIM** lies flat on the floor, starfished, whispering, “Worth it…”  **BILL** is pacing like a lawyer who just lost a case on a technicality.  **GILLEY** beams like the dictator of his own failed state.

**JEFF** sips his flat soda. Watching. Cataloguing.

**BILL** (still muttering)
I want an appeals process. A recount. At least an independent auditor.

**KATHY**
You lost. Take the L and your dignity.

**JIM** (groans)
I lost my dignity around event three.

**GILLEY**
Correction: you weaponized it. That’s why you placed third.

**JEREMY** (half-dead, but smug)
And I still won. I always win.

**BILL**
Only because you drank a liquid crime scene.

**GILLEY** (pointing the broken mic stand at Jeff)
Final word goes to Judge Evans.

All eyes shift. **JEFF** lowers his soda can. Shrugs.

**JEFF**
We don’t even know if we’ll get the other tickets yet.

(beat)
And if we do? Today’s rankings go in the trash with the pizza box.

Everyone groans, but nobody argues. Because he’s right.

**KATHY** (softly, but with a grin)
Still…it was a hell of a game.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It didn’t matter who “won.” It never would. What mattered was six kids, in a garage, turning sweat and boredom into something worth remembering.

**JEFF (V.O.) (CONT’D)**
We thought we were fighting for tickets.  Really, we were proving we’d do anything — anything at all — to keep this band alive.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It was stupid. It was sweaty. It was unfair.  And it was one of the best afternoons of my life.

---

## **EXT. LOADING DOCK – LATE MORNING – FOUR DAYS LATER**

The asphalt shimmers. Heat bends the air into waves. Diesel engines idle low, coughing fumes.

**JEREMY EVANS** wrestles a dolly stacked with two full kegs up the warped ramp, sweat dripping into his eyes. His shirt is plastered, his hands raw.

From the shade, **FRANK** — beer rep, chain necklace, permanent smirk — steps forward sipping a Diet Pepsi like it’s bourbon on the Riviera.

**FRANK**
Evans!

**JEREMY** doesn’t answer. Just leans into the dolly like it’s the last push of his life.

**FRANK** (calling)
Don’t give me that “working man” look. I got a question.

Jeremy parks the dolly, wipes his forehead with the hem of his shirt, squints.

**JEREMY**
What.

**FRANK**
When the hell you getting your band into WO Wrights?

Jeremy blinks.

**JEREMY**
The bar?

**FRANK** (calling after him, grinning)
“WO Wright’s’ll spin anything, man. Guided by Voices, Brainiac, that noise-punk duo who only own one guitar string. They broke ’em all.”

Jeremy just stares.

**JEREMY**
How do you even—

**FRANK** (cutting him off, grinning)
Bobby from routing. Plays your demo tape every damn day. Rewinds it, cranks it, rewinds it again. Whole office sounds like a chainsaw having feelings.

Jeremy’s face twists — caught somewhere between pride and embarrassment.

**JEREMY**
It was recorded on a boom box. In Gilley’s garage.

**FRANK**
Yeah. And it *still* gets heads bobbing between invoices. Which means it’s either genius…or total garbage.

(beat, leaning in, mock-serious)
But either way, man, we’re sick of guessing what you sound like under all that static.

**JEREMY** stays still, sweat dripping off his jawline.

**JEREMY (V.O.)**
We thought no one heard us. That the noise never left the garage.

(beat)
Turns out somebody was listening. Through the worst speakers in Dayton.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S KITCHEN – EARLY EVENING – JULY 1990**

The linoleum’s yellowed. The overhead light stutters with a mosquito caught behind the globe, thumping a slow, idiot rhythm. Six bodies pressed tight around a sticky Formica table cluttered with pizza bones, cigarette butts, a half-gutted answering machine, and a *Dayton Yellow Pages* stained with root beer.

**GILLEY**—barefoot, shirtless, sweatprint on the back of his jeans, mirrored gas station sunglasses despite the twilight—leans on an elbow, chewing a toothpick with the slow menace of a man who’s never been punched for it.

He flips the phone book, stabs a listing with his finger.

**GILLEY**
Alright, ladies and weirdos, tonight we go pro.

**KATHY**, sleeves shoved to the wrist, picks cheese off cold pizza and doesn’t look up.

**KATHY**
You’re calling a dive bar in Beavercreek.

**GILLEY**
Correction: I’m securing a *high-profile regional engagement*. Think vision, Espich.

**BILL**, cross-legged on the counter, twirling a synth cable like rosary beads.

**BILL**
You mean cold-calling a bar from a rotary, in your mom’s kitchen, at dinner time.

**JEFF**, already peeling the label off his second Sun Drop.

**JEFF**
Do you even know what you’re going to say, or is this another improv disaster?

**GILLEY** (Grins, wild)
Nope. That’s what makes it *art*, Jeff.

He yanks the corded tan rotary off the hook, the phone’s plastic shell grimy with years of hands and spilled soup. He dials with a showman’s flourish, spinning the dial, and leans back so far his chair squeaks a warning.

RING. RING. RING. A pause.
On the other end: the sound of a man who has never not been smoking a cigarette.

**BAR GUY** (V.O., raspy, bored)
Yeah?

**GILLEY**
This is Jeremy Gilley, frontman-slash-booking for Arcadia Burning.
(The band freezes, Bill’s eyes narrow—*what name?*)
We’re an original act out of Bethel, heavy on soul-grit, low-end, and unapologetic volume. Calling to see if you’ve got a slot for live music.

**BAR GUY** (V.O.)
You a cover band or what?

**GILLEY** (With no hesitation)
Could be. We’re versatile. We got hits nobody knows yet.

**BAR GUY** (V.O.)
Thursdays only. You bring a draw?

**GILLEY**
Oh, sure. Big following. College crowd, Air Force brats, some local sales reps, Frank. Frank says we’re the best band this side of 675.

**BAR GUY** (V.O.)
You got a demo or just your balls?

**GILLEY**
Cassette. Raw as hell. You’ll feel it in your teeth.

**BAR GUY** (V.O.)
Drop it with Big Mike behind the taps. Tell him Terry says put it on after darts.
(Beat. Hangs up mid-sentence.)

**GILLEY** sets the phone down like he’s just closed an arms deal in a war zone. He turns to the band with a predator’s grin.

**GILLEY**
Gentlemen—and Kathy—behold. I have secured our destiny.

**KATHY**
Did you just *name* the band without asking?

**GILLEY**
Arcadia Burning. It’s evocative. It’s grand. It sounds like we own leather pants.

**JIM** (deadpan, rolling his sticks)
Sounds like a Christian metal act from Cedarville.

**BILL**
Or the world’s worst case of heartburn.

**JEFF**
Do we even *have* a demo with that name on it?

**JEREMY**
I got one in my Volvo. Sharpie on the shell: ‘Garage – Side A.’ B-side is mostly Bill’s failed synth solos and Kathy threatening to quit.

**GILLEY**
Perfect. That’s the legend. That’s how we start.

Kathy bites her tongue, eyes on the demo tape like it’s an unexploded grenade.
The fan in the window stirs the smoke. No one moves, not yet.

**GILLEY** (softer, almost reverent)
First gig, kids. Even if it’s for Big Mike and three drunk regulars and a bowl of stale pretzels. This is it.

The room holds its breath. Somewhere outside, a jet from Wright-Patt claws across the sky, the drone rattling glasses in the sink.

*Fade out on six kids in a shitty kitchen, daring the world to listen.*

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S KITCHEN – CONTINUOUS – JULY 1990**

The rotary phone dangles, spinning slow on its cord, nearly pulling the base off the wall. A cold crust of pizza sits on the table, stiff as a corpse. Kathy’s knees are pulled up to her chin, picking at a sticker peeling off her guitar case. Jeremy drums the table with bitten-down nails, eyes glazed with the boredom of a thousand bad nights. Nobody’s looking at Gilley now, but he’s looking at all of them—smug, but the kind that cracks when you poke it.

**KATHY** (Too sharp)
Arcadia Burning? Really, Gilley?

**BILL** (Snorts)
Sounds like a vape shop that exploded.

**JIM**
Worse. It’s like an alt-weekly poetry contest—everybody’s wounded, nobody’s published.

**JEREMY**
I swear I saw a band called Arcadia Burning open for a cover band at the fair. Their set ended with the sound guy unplugging them.

**GILLEY** (defensive, spreading hands)
It was on the spot, okay? We needed something.

**JEFF**
And what you picked was “bad.” Not even ironically bad. Just, like, shit.

**KATHY**
We need a name. We need an identity. Not just…whatever fell out of your mouth.

**BILL**
Alright, brainstorm. Go. Quick. No pride, no points for subtlety.

**JIM**
Free Beer.

**BILL**
Guaranteed walkouts after the first set.

**JEREMY**
Feedback Loop?

**JEFF**
That’s a threat, not a band.

**KATHY**
Half Deaf and Broke.

(A pause. It lands. A little too true.)

**JIM**
That’s just autobiography.

**BILL**
Basement Gospel?

**GILLEY**
You ever seen a basement in this house?

**JEFF**
Gospel? In Bethel? Try again.

**KATHY**
Laundry Fire.

**JIM**
That’s what happened to my only suit.

**JEREMY**
Misspent Youth?

**BILL**
Probably trademarked by three punk bands and a failing youth group.

**GILLEY**
What if it’s just *stupid*? Something nobody can ignore because it’s so bad it bores into your skull.

**KATHY**
Like?

**GILLEY**
The Broken Spokes.

**JEREMY**
Americana Cosplay.

**BILL**
Tympanoplasty.

**JEFF**
Fuck off, Bill.

**JIM**
Hot Lunch.

**ALL (in unison, instantly)**
No.

(Silence. Just the hum of the fridge and the old clock over the stove. Someone’s foot taps nervously, desperate for movement.)

**KATHY**
Let’s be honest. What do we sound like?

**JEFF**
A bar fight in a pawn shop.

**BILL**
A synth falling down a flight of stairs.

**JEREMY**
Jim screaming like he found a possum in his kick drum.

**JIM**
That was one time.

**GILLEY**
We sound like a prom night transmission failing at a four-way stop.

**KATHY**
We sound like Ohio when the factories close and nobody cares.

(A long, messy beat.)

**JEREMY**
Cauterize the Midwest.

**ALL**
No.

(And then, almost quietly, tired but determined—)

**KATHY**
What about This Is a Brick?

(The table stills. Even Gilley doesn’t talk for a second.)

**BILL**
What does that even mean?

**KATHY**
It means no metaphor. No apology. Just: *Here. It lands. It hurts. It doesn’t need a fucking reason.*

**JEFF**
That’s…weirdly honest. And honest is better than good.

**JIM**
It’s ugly. It’s stupid. It’s us.

**GILLEY**
At least it isn’t Arcadia Fucking Burning.

**JEREMY**
So we’re This Is a Brick now? Until we regret it?

**KATHY**
Until someone has a worse idea.

(Silence. The hum of the refrigerator, the creak of an old chair. No lightning bolt, no secret handshake. Just a band, exhausted and hungry, naming itself out of spite and surrender.)

**BILL**
There it is. This Is a Brick. If it sucks, at least we’ll break something.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
That’s how it started. Not a dream. Not a plan. Just a moment of inertia breaking, and a name nobody could out-stubborn.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S KITCHEN – EVENING – JULY 1990**

The overhead bulb hums. Shadows swim on the cracked linoleum. The air is thick with warm Mountain Dew and old garlic bread. On the table: a scatter of receipts, napkins, torn notebook pages, all scrawled with failed band names, grease-stained and crossed out with bitter strokes. The silence is total. Six bodies, slumped and unmoving, every joke exhausted. Not even the flies have the heart to circle anymore.

**JIM**
This is purgatory.

**BILL**
It’s not even purgatory. It’s the holding cell outside, with “Radio Free Europe” looping on a blown speaker.

**KATHY**
We are six bodies in a Midwestern kitchen, dying by inches under the weight of indecision and Gilley’s ego.

**GILLEY**
Look, I’m sorry for Arcadia Burning. I had five seconds. I panicked.

**JEREMY**
You used all of them poorly.

**JEFF**
This isn’t even a band meeting. We’re just torturing each other with bad nouns.

**BILL**
Thunder Voucher. That’s not a band. That’s a scam.

**JIM**
Crate of Dad. That’s just trauma.

**KATHY**
This Is a Brick sounded right for a minute, then felt like self-harm.

**GILLEY**
I still think Hot Lunch could work—

**ALL**
NO.

(Silence. Kathy picks at the crust on a Little Caesars box. Jeremy’s head is in his hands. Bill draws pentagrams around a grease stain.)

**JEREMY**
I just want this to end. I want the sentence over. I want the Governor to pardon us. Or Jesus to walk in and say, “My children, you have suffered enough.”

**BILL**
I’d take Mr. Haddix. Remember?

(Shrugs)
He cried at the Chernobyl slideshow. I’d trust that man with my life.

**JEFF**
He had a soul. He’d know how to get us out of this.

**JIM**
Time served. That’s what I want. I want out.

(Long beat. Nobody looks up.)

**KATHY** (quiet, not even meaning to say it)
Well then…what about Commuted?

It hangs there.  Nobody laughs. Nobody groans. It’s a silence you can feel in your ribs.

**JEFF**
Commuted.

**BILL**
Like the sentence cut short.

**JEREMY**
Like what we’ve been begging for.

**KATHY**
It fits.

**JIM**
It’s got a ring to it. Like the sound a cell door makes.

**GILLEY**
It’s…right. Fuck. It’s actually right.

**BILL**
And it’s ours.

They look at each other. For once, nobody jokes. Something lands—solid, real, undeniable.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
We didn’t find it. It found us, right at the edge.  That was the whole point.  *Commuted.* Not just our sentence—our band, our sound, our only chance to get free.

**(Beat. The hum of the bulb, the world outside indifferent. For a moment, they believe.)**

---

## **INT. KATHY’S BASEMENT – NIGHT – JULY 1990**

The walls sweat with condensation. There’s a patch of black mold blooming behind the water heater and the floor is sticky with spilled Coke and guitar polish. Extension cords snake under sagging posters of bands that never played Dayton. In the far corner, a box fan groans against a pile of laundry.  Six bodies sprawl in the dim, huddled around a battered Brother word processor the color of old teeth. The monitor flickers, toxic green, throwing shadows over their faces.

Bill—shirt off, knees up—hunts and pecks at the keyboard with the intensity of a cryptographer defusing a bomb.  Kathy sits cross-legged on a blanket, X-Acto knife in one hand, glue stick in the other, surrounded by cut-up *Rolling Stone* covers and failed attempts at a logo.  Jeremy’s in a camp chair, taping flyers to the wall, biting a hangnail.  Jim’s got his legs up on a crate, eating cheese curls with orange fingers and a deranged patience.  Gilley paces, trying to light a cigarette from a dead lighter, sunglasses still on indoors.  Jeff watches the word processor like it’s a heart monitor.

**JIM** (chewing)
Should we even bother with a logo? Or is the name enough?

**KATHY**
We don’t even have a setlist. Or a van. Or a clue.

**GILLEY**
We need an *image*. Punk bands had the safety pin, The Stones had the tongue, Zeppelin had…whatever. We need something—like a brick breaking a gavel.

**JEFF**
A flaming gavel is a lawsuit waiting to happen.

**JEREMY**
What if we just keep it brutalist?

(He tears a strip of white tape, holds it up)
Name. Date. Venue. Done.

**BILL**
Fine. No frills. No pyrotechnics.

(mutters as he types, green letters blooming on screen)
“LIVE AT W.O. WRIGHTS – THURSDAY – DEBUT SHOW –”

(He squints, tongue between teeth)
“—OF COMMUTED.”

He jabs the print button. The Brother whirs to life—dot matrix screaming, the stench of hot ink and ozone filling the basement. The first page shudders out, ragged and askew.

Gilley snatches it. Everyone leans in.

Across the top, in bold block font:
“COMUTED”

A beat.

**KATHY**
No?

**JIM**
We lost a goddamn consonant.

**JEFF**
Bill!

**BILL**
It’s there!

(stares, sweating)
Wait—shit. No, it isn’t. The ‘m’ is just…hanging out.

**JEREMY**
It needs a roommate. Or a lawyer.

**BILL**
How many did I print?

(CUT TO:)

---

**MONTAGE: THE FLYERS GO OUT**

- A “COMUTED LIVE!” flyer duct-taped to the grimy front door of W.O. Wrights, below a faded sign that says “NO OUTSIDE FOOD. NO FIGHTS.”
- A flyer tacked up in the Tecumseh High teacher’s lounge—right next to a “LOST CAT: ANSWERS TO STAN” and an abstinence pledge card.
- One crammed on the bulletin board at the Speedway, wedged between a moon pie coupon and a pyramid scheme for Amway.
- A flyer stapled to a telephone pole on Route 40, drooping in the wind, right below a missing dog poster and a phone sex hotline.
- Another taped inside the men’s stall at Bill’s Place—at eye-level, right above “CALL TRACY FOR A GOOD TIME” and a faded Sharpie drawing of a penis.
- Jeremy tossing one through the cracked window of a maroon Cavalier, parked sideways at the Dairy Dream.
- Jim shoving a handful into the magazine rack at the laundromat, under a stack of tattered *People*.

**BACK TO BASEMENT:**

The band stares at their mistake.  Gilley lights his cigarette off the stove burner. Kathy just laughs, a snort, then a groan, then covers her face.

**KATHY**
We’re going to be legends. Legends who can’t spell.

**JEFF**
At least we didn’t go with Hot Lunch.

They pass the flyer around, fingers greasy, eyes tired, no one wanting to be the one to throw it away.  The dot matrix keeps printing, relentless, grinding out their future—one typo at a time.

---
## **INT. KATHY’S BASEMENT – LATER**

A sticky orange extension cord buzzes against the cinderblock wall. The printer’s gone silent, leaving the air thick with the stink of hot plastic and regret. The flyers are stacked in a lopsided pile, “COMUTED” glaring up at them in pixelated defiance. Everyone’s sitting on the floor, backs against the dryer, shoes off, eyes hollow. There’s a sense they’ve all aged five years in the last hour.

Gilley flips through the flyers, shaking his head, then holds one up like it’s evidence at a crime scene.

**GILLEY**
So…what’s our name again?

**KATHY** (sighing)
Apparently? *Comuted.* We’re Ohio’s best misspelling.

**JIM**
Sounds like something you plug into a Packard Bell to hear static and disappointment.

**JEREMY**
Or a Transformer they made out of leftover parts. “Behold, I am Comuted—destroyer of spelling bees.”

Bill snickers, finally. Even Kathy lets herself smile, just a little, head lolling back against the old washing machine.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
The name didn’t matter. Not really. The songs mattered. The sweat, the hours, the bruises, the screaming into a box fan to drown out the world. That was us. Six half-grown animals in a damp basement, too tired to quit.

(beat)
But now? We had a gig.

And that, for a second, is enough.

---

# **CHAPTER FOUR: THE GIG**

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – BACK HALLWAY – THURSDAY NIGHT – 8:21 P.M. – JULY 1990**

Fluorescents flicker overhead, turning everyone’s skin a shade of sick. The hallway smells like fryer oil, spilled Coors Light, and a decade’s worth of mopped-in disappointment. The jukebox in the bar is playing “Simple Man” so loud you can’t tell if it’s nostalgia or threat. There’s a puddle by the door that nobody wants to identify.

The band huddles, a tangle of cords, nervous fingers, and thrift-store jackets. Sweat halos every armpit. Jeremy’s bouncing a quarter off his amp case. Kathy picks at her thumbnail until it bleeds. Jim drums a nervous, uneven tattoo on his snare case. Jeff stares at the setlist, mouthing lyrics he already knows. Bill fiddles with the synth power supply, praying for a miracle. Gilley tries to look taller than he is, sunglasses perched on his head, but his hands won’t stop shaking.

Enter **BIG MIKE**: mustache like a middle finger, arms as thick as the fryer basket, T-shirt tucked and gut unapologetic, a clipboard scarred with names of bands that never came back. He sizes them up with one eye half-closed, smoke drifting in lazy loops above his head.

**BIG MIKE**
Alright.

(He points at **JEREMY** with the authority of a man who’s seen every scam in the tri-county area.)

You’re the one dropped off that tape last week. Thought about using it to scrape gum off the bar. Figured it was another Bon Jovi or “Party Train” act—kids from Tipp who want to be loud and go home early.

(He leans in, voice dropping, conspiratorial.)
But then I threw it on after darts. Swear to God, the room didn’t empty. Not right away, anyway.

He squints, looking for the right insult.

**BIG MIKE** (CONT’D)
It’s…weird. Not bad, just—hell, if Tom Waits and Devo fucked in a Wendy’s bathroom, that’s what came out. And Tommy? Tommy hates everything. But he stayed. Bought another pitcher. So maybe you’re onto something. Or maybe they were just too drunk to leave.

(Scratches his neck, squints again.)

**BIG MIKE** (CONT’D)
Here’s the deal. You keep fifty people in this shithole after ten o’clock—nobody bails for the patio, nobody ghosts for a smoke, nobody goes home early—and I’ll throw you an extra fifty bucks.

**JIM**
That’s like…nine dollars a head.

**BIG MIKE**
Yeah, so keep 'em in. Play loud, play fast, no ballads, no slow-dance bullshit. And don’t try to get cute with the covers—keep it classic.

**GILLEY**
What if they wanna dance?

**BIG MIKE**
Nobody dances here. Not since the VFW Christmas party in ’87, and that ended with two teeth in the urinal.

**GILLEY**
Then they aren't friends of mine!

**BIG MIKE**
Just keep it hot. If it gets weird, own it. If it gets ugly, make it music.

(Points with his cigarette)
Sound guy’s drunk as hell. If you touch the monitor knob, you’ll get shocked. Not “static shock” shocked—*hospital trip* shocked.

(Pauses for effect)
Break a leg.

**KATHY**
Wait, are you serious about the knob?

**BIG MIKE**
Break a leg. But not on the stairs—insurance don’t kick in until ten.

He turns, already lighting another smoke, writing them off before they’ve even begun.

The band stands, blinking, in a silence that isn’t hope—it’s the long, low pressure before a tornado.  You can hear the crowd, somewhere out there: glasses clinking, boots stomping, laughter with a serrated edge. A room full of people waiting to eat you alive.

**JEREMY**
Fifty bucks.

**JEFF**
Fifty people.

**GILLEY**
We got this.
(But he’s chewing his cheek, not fooling anybody.)

**KATHY**
Only if we don’t suck.

**JIM**
Or die.

**BILL**
Same difference.

They look at each other—kids dressed up as a band, holding their breath, hoping the floor doesn’t collapse beneath them.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Nine o’clock. Fifty people. One shot.  No pressure.

(beat)
All the pressure in the world.

*SMASH CUT to the stage lights—too bright, too hot, and a microphone that smells like beer and someone else’s spit.*

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – 9:03 P.M. – JULY 1990**

Stage lights too hot, sweat crawling down their spines. The band is running out of energy and time—Gilley’s shirtless, chest blotchy with nerves. Kathy’s hair is stuck to her face, eyes wild, fingers moving like she’s exorcising demons one string at a time. Jeremy’s locked into his fretboard, jaw clenched, working a riff like he’s wringing money from stone. Bill’s synth howls and spits, barely in tune. Jim’s beating the drums so hard he’s bent a stick, veins popping on his neck.

Jeff is counting. Always counting.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Okay. Focus, you fucker. Fifty. You need fifty. Don’t lose the count.

Every song is a numbers game.
He sees—

- Three airmen at the bar, hunched and suspicious, nodding to the beat but never looking at the stage.
- A table of Wright State kids, mostly flirting, a couple watching, one passed out.
- Cowboy hat guy, knee-bouncing so hard his boots squeak.
- Two bartenders, bored, arms crossed, lips tight.
- A woman in a leather jacket, smoking like it’s her religion, eyes promising violence.

He mouths numbers between bass lines. His shirt is glued to his back.

**JEFF**
Five…ten…twelve…shit, did I count that guy twice? Fourteen…

(panicking)
Twenty-two…twenty-eight…thirty-three…

Kathy shoots him a look, never missing a note.

**KATHY** (mouths, urgent)
Count faster!

**JEFF** (whispered, frantic)
Thirty-seven…thirty-nine…forty…forty-three…

Big Mike leans against the bar, arms folded, face unreadable. Judgment radiates off him like static.
A pitcher shatters somewhere. Nobody cares.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
C’mon, c’mon, c’mon. Jesus, grow a body.

**JEFF**
Forty-four…forty-six…fuck. Forty-seven.

Kathy inches over mid-solo, breathing hard, hair wild.

**KATHY**
How bad?

**JEFF**
We’re three short.

**JIM**
I’m playing like I’m summoning the dead over here!

**BILL**
If anyone dies tonight, at least we’ll have a full crowd.

**GILLEY**
Somebody block the exits!

Jim lets out a manic laugh—too loud, too close to tears.

**JIM**
Maybe we can pay the barflies to multiply.

**GILLEY**
Is that guy by the pool table moving?

**JIM**
Nope. That’s the jukebox guy. Same shirt as before.

**GILLEY**
Motherfucker.

---

### **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – SIDE STAGE / HALLWAY – 9:09 P.M.**

The song collapses into feedback. The crowd is thinning. Jeff’s counting with panic in his eyes. Gilley’s chewing his lip bloody. Kathy’s voice is going raw.

Jeremy, sweating bullets, bolts offstage, guitar squealing as he drops it. He lunges for the payphone, slams in a quarter with shaking hands.

**JEREMY** (into phone, rapid-fire, near hysterical)
Mom? MOM? I need you to get in the car right now. Bring Josiah. Bring Joslyn. Bring the cat if you have to. Just—warm bodies. W.O. Wright’s. Twelve miles. Please.

(beat — listening, desperate)
I’ll do the dishes. Two weeks. Just—hurry!

He slams the receiver down and sprints back toward the stage.

*Back on stage: the crowd, the band, the sound of failure hungry like a wolf.*

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – MOMENTS LATER**

The song’s ending, applause barely registers. The band is sweating through their clothes, caught somewhere between comedy and crisis. Jeff is counting on his fingers. Gilley’s wringing the mic stand, eyes flicking toward the door like he can will family into existence. Kathy’s wiping her forehead with her sleeve, not caring if she smears mascara. The house lights burn every flaw into the open.

**KATHY** (half-pleading, half-accusatory)
What did you just do?

**JEREMY**
Used the bugle horn to summon the Evans Cavalry.

**JIM**
Oh God. We’re that band.

**JEFF**
Oh yes. This is the future. All ages, all desperation.

**GILLEY**
God bless your emotionally manipulative mother.

(beat)
Seriously. If she saves us, she gets a songwriting credit.

**BILL**
We’re really gonna count your mom and siblings as paying audience?

**KATHY**
Hell yes. If they walk in that door, they’re worth more than every half-buzzed airman in here.

**GILLEY**
Did you just sell your soul for crowd control?

**JEREMY**
No. Worse. I sold my chore schedule.

(two fingers up)
Two weeks, no complaints. Plus Josiah gets DJ rights in the car.

**KATHY**
How many are coming?

**JEREMY**
Three. Mom. Josiah. Joslyn. That gets us to the line, if they hustle.

**JEFF**
If they show up before ten, it’s gospel. Big Mike can count to fifty, but he can’t subtract.

**JIM**
God, I hope they make it. I can’t go home and tell my dad we lost the gig over three bodies.

**BILL**
We’re officially the first band rescued by the Ohio Department of Transportation.

Gilley laughs, but it’s half-mad.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Twelve miles. One Hyundai. Three warm bodies. That was our Hail Mary.

(beat; voice lower, bitter)
It wasn’t music. It wasn’t magic. It was survival.

(beat)
That’s what passed for rock and roll.

---

### **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – 9:49 P.M.**

Every amp is howling. Sweat pours off the band like they’re burning off sins. Gilley’s shirt is gone, his hair sticking to his face. Kathy’s strings are frayed, fingers blistered, eyes wild. Jeremy’s pick hand is cramping, but he’s not letting go. Bill’s synth is glitching, hissing and shrieking, pure noise made holy. Jim’s drumming like the rent’s due at midnight.

Jeff is still counting, desperate, doomed.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Forty-seven…forty-eight, if that bathroom guy’s alive…oh, Jesus, come on…

And then:  The air brakes hiss. Twice. Louder than the amps.

---

### **EXT. PARKING LOT – CONTINUOUS**

Two battered blue school buses, **U.S. AIR FORCE** stenciled in with field chalk, groan into the lot.  Doors bang open. Floodlights spill onto boots, fatigues, and faces red from a day spent hauling jet engines and grandstands.  And then: **an army of airmen**, tumbling out in packs, whooping, bumping shoulders, half still reeking of sunblock and hot dog grease.

---

### **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – FRONT BAR – CONTINUOUS**

The door slams against the wall. In pours the storm.  **BIG MIKE** steps out, half a shot of Wild Turkey in one hand, beer towel in the other, jaw going slack as camo and combat boots stampede the bar.

**BIG MIKE** (soft, shellshocked)
Oh Jesus. They came back.

Airmen fill the floor—at the bar, the pool table, the bathrooms, pressed tight at the front of the stage. The energy spikes so hard the glasses rattle. The jukebox guy cheers. Even the old-timers give up their stools.

**SrA #1** (pointing at the stage)
Hey! These the cassette kids!?

**SrA #2**
Frank said it’s like Black Sabbath got electrocuted in a carwash!

**SrA #3**
LOUDER, YOU FUCKERS!

---

### **INT. STAGE – SAME TIME**

For a second, the band just *stares*, gaping. Gilley’s mouth actually drops open.

**GILLEY**
How many is that?

**JEFF**
I stopped at a hundred and four. I’m just trying not to piss myself.

**JIM** (over the mic, breathless)
We’re so far past quota it’s biblical.

**KATHY**
I count 147. Not including the guy making out with the jukebox. He’s got stamina.

**BILL**
He counts twice. That's devotion.

**JEREMY** (laughing, high on adrenaline)
Let’s go. Let’s make it worth it.

Kathy grins at Gilley. Gilley whoops. Jim hammers the snare like a shot fired at the moon.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Turns out we didn’t need Mom. Turns out we didn’t need a miracle—just a couple hundred airmen and the wildest luck in Ohio. Turns out, for one night, we were the right band in the wrong bar at exactly the right fucking time.

(beat)
One hundred forty-seven strong.  And for once, nobody was counting anymore.

*The band launches into the next song.  The sound is chaos, joy, terror, the beginning of everything.*

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – 9:51 P.M. – JULY 1990**

The floor is bouncing. The walls sweat. Airmen are howling, college kids pressed in tight, the entire place stinks of cheap beer and a hundred gallons of adrenaline. The band is running on nothing but nerves, nicotine, and the will not to choke.  They crash out of a song, feedback still ringing, breaths ragged, eyes wild.

SFX: A table goes down in the back. No one cares.

A bear of a man in camo steps forward, owning the room with every stomp.  **TECH SERGEANT FRALEY**—mustache bristling, uniform half-untucked, voice like a warhead.

**TECH SERGEANT FRALEY** (pointing, booming)
HEY! YOU GUYS PLAY QUIET RIOT?

Jeff freezes, looks at the band, then back at the sergeant—half-hoping this is a joke, half-knowing he’ll die if he says no.

**JEFF**
You mean “*Cum On Feel the Noize*”?  It's Slade.

**TECH SERGEANT FRALEY** (oblivious)
HELL YEAH I MEAN “COME ON FEEL THE NOISE”!

The crowd’s attention sharpens. Now it’s a dare.  Gilley’s eyes bug. Kathy’s mouth splits into a wicked grin. Jeremy fakes confidence. Jim looks like he’s about to tear the kit apart. Bill is already plotting keyboard blasphemy.

**JEFF** (shrugs, surrendering)
Anybody know the riff?

**KATHY**
I think I do. I think the whole state of Ohio does.

**GILLEY**
They’re all just power chords, right?

(to Kathy)
Hit it.

**JEREMY**
Give me a bar, I’ll fake the rest.  I kinda remember the fake book chords.

**JIM**
I’m just gonna hit everything like I’m pissed at my dad.

**BILL**
I’ll make it sound like church in hell.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
We weren’t a cover band. We weren’t even a *good* band. But for three and a half minutes, we became a cover band with zero pride and less dignity. And it was beautiful.

## **ONSTAGE – SECONDS LATER**

Kathy tears into the riff—sloppy, loud, two strings at a time, but the crowd *knows*.  Gilley shouts the chorus, voice already shot, but nobody cares. Jeremy fakes solos with pure volume. Jim makes the snare sound like it’s being exorcised. Bill leans on one key, lets it wail like sirens. Jeff anchors it all, bass thrumming in his bones.

The place *explodes*.

**A1C JODONOVIC**
YOOOOOOOOOOO!

A girl climbs on a chair and screams. Airmen pump fists, slam pitchers, spill everywhere. Tech Sergeant Fraley loses his mind, windmilling his arm like he’s back in 1984. Someone crowd surfs on a folding chair.

**GILLEY** (into mic, laughing, untamed)
For the Air Show crew—may you never have to hear us again!

The band hurls through the song like it’s the last train out of town.

---

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Was it tight? Fuck no. Was it good? Not even close.  Was it the loudest, dumbest, sweatiest thing this place had ever survived?  Goddamn right.

(beat)
That’s when we stopped being a joke, even to ourselves.

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – 9:53 P.M.**

The band is at DEFCON 1. The crowd is a mob—airmen chanting, glasses shattering, beer sloshing everywhere. Gilley’s lost his shirt, his mind, and maybe his future vocal cords. He struts across the plywood like Caesar returning from Gaul.

**GILLEY** (howling into the mic)
“GIRLS — ROCK YOUR BOOOOYS!”

The bar erupts—an unholy choir of drunks, airmen pounding tables, a fistfight starting by the pool table, the bartender just trying to stay vertical.

In the middle of the chaos:  **The door creaks.**

And in walks **MRS. EVANS**—buttoned-up, unfazed, arm hooked around a battered purse. Josiah and Joslyn step in behind, wide-eyed, carrying the look of kids just dropped off at the circus and realizing the animals run the place. Mrs. Evans takes in the room—riot, noise, spilled beer, an airman twerking on the jukebox.

**Just as Gilley screams again:**

**GILLEY**
“GIIIIIIIRLS — ROCK YOUR—”

He spots her.  Everything in his face goes slack. He actually *blinks*.  The music hiccups, stutters, *doesn’t stop*.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
…uh…boys.

**ONSTAGE:**
Jeremy is frozen, hands mid-chord, mouth open in horror.  Kathy’s half-laughing, half-terrified.

**KATHY**
Is that—?
(points)

**JEFF**
Yup.

**JIM**
I didn’t know Moms could teleport.

**BILL**
She looks like she’s about to audit your ass.

---

**ON THE FLOOR:**
Mrs. Evans glides through the drunken sea like Moses parting the Redneck Sea. She doesn’t scold. She just *surveys*—judging, affirming, enduring. At the back wall, she presses her gum onto the glowing **Budweiser On Tap** sign—firm, deliberate. A private ritual. Every Evans knows: Bud is the enemy.

She turns to Josiah and Joslyn, voice pure Midwest steel:

**MRS. EVANS**
Well. At least they’re not getting booed off the stage.

She settles in, unmoved by the circus.

**BACK ONSTAGE:**
Gilley, voice gone, tries to rally.

**GILLEY** (raspy, deadpan)
That was our Quiet Riot cover. We call it “Slightly Alarmed Riot.” If you’re feeling awkward, you’re doing it right.

The crowd laughs, cheers, sloshes beer.

Jeremy mutters, half-laughing, half-mortified:

**JEREMY**
She’s going to tell the whole family. Every. Single. Thanksgiving.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
The place was packed. The music was loud. The airmen were wild.  And Mom? Mom saw the whole damn thing.

(beat)
*That’s how you know you’re a real band.*

**FADE OUT ON:**
Mrs. Evans—arms crossed, half-smiling, watching her son and his disaster family become *something more than a punchline*.

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – NEAR THE BACK WALL – 9:48 P.M.**

Mrs. Evans scans the room like a general appraising a defeated city—PTA discipline warring with a mother’s ancient patience. The air is thick with the stench of beer, fryer grease, and a kind of Midwestern despair you can’t scrub out with bleach.

She surveys the chaos:

– One airman snoring in a booth, camouflage hat tilted like a gravestone.
– A pitcher of Busch Light upending itself into the felt of the pool table, ignored by everyone.
– Gilley, shirtless and shining, standing on a battered amp, arms up like he’s conquering Rome, pupils huge, pure sweat and delusion.
– Jeremy, onstage, shrinking behind his guitar, wishing he was a potted plant.
– Jim throwing drumsticks like grenades.
– Bill, hunched over his synth, coaxing out sounds that could be music or the beginning of an alien invasion.

Mrs. Evans sighs, low and judgmental, as only a woman who’s seen it all can. She turns to her children, her voice a controlled detonation.

**MRS. EVANS**
Sunday Bloody Sunday, Jeremy.

**JEREMY** (still shell-shocked)
What?

**MRS. EVANS**
The scene. The smell. Your friend screaming about rocking boys.

(beat, eyes narrowing at Gilley)
Thirty minutes. Tops. This place is appalling.

Joslyn, 13, checks her nails, deadpan.

**JOSLYN**
Is the keyboard guy okay? Is he having a seizure or just pretending?

Josiah, 11, beaming, head on a swivel.

**JOSIAH**
This rules!  The Macho Man can make a synthesizer talk like a robot!

Mrs. Evans makes a show of shifting her purse away from anything sticky, then stalks to the back wall, hunting for the least contaminated chair—settling with imperial posture in a seat that may or may not have been a crime scene.

She sets her purse on her lap, folds her arms, and fixes the band with the withering gaze of someone who has *absolutely nothing to prove*.

**MRS. EVANS** (pulling out her phone)
Thirty minutes. I’m setting a timer. Don’t make me stay a second longer.

---

**ONSTAGE:**

Jeremy looks like he’s about to sink into the floor, face burning.  Kathy leans in, grinning.

**KATHY**
Dude. Your mom just “Sunday Bloody Sunday’d” you in front of a hundred airmen.  That’s biblical.

**JEFF**
That’s a Geneva Convention violation.

**GILLEY**
We gotta play faster, before she files a report.

**JIM**
We gotta play better, before she writes a letter to the manager.

**BILL**
It’s too late. The review is called *life*.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
A full house. Airmen moshing. Family on the clock. Thirty minutes until Mrs. Evans called time of death.

(beat)
The show was on.

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – SIDE OF STAGE – 9:51 P.M.**

The band huddles, **GILLEY** dripping with adrenaline, **JEREMY** pale from maternal impact, **JEFF** tuning without looking up. Out in the crowd, Mrs. Evans sits stone-faced, sipping from a plastic cup and eyeing the crowd like she’s waiting for a reason to call a health inspector.

**JEREMY** (urgent)
She wants Sunday Bloody Sunday.

**KATHY**
As in…the actual song?

**JIM**
I know the drum pattern. It’s just snare death march meets Irish angst.

**GILLEY**
I know the vocals. Sort of. I’ll figure it out. My range is…flexible.

**BILL** (eyes wide)
Guys.

(beat)
This song doesn’t need synth.

**JEFF**
Yeah, but you do.

**BILL**
It’s a U2 protest anthem. It’s all drums, guitar, and that stupid, perfect delay riff. It’s dry. It’s angry. It’s—it’s allergic to keyboards.

**KATHY**
Can you make it weird?

**BILL**
I can always make it weird.

**GILLEY**
Then do it. Channel some Eno-at-3am shit. Give it ghosts or something.

**BILL**
Ghosts I can do.

(beat)
I’ll hang in the background like a synth-shaped war crime.

**JIM**
Welcome to the theme of the night.

---

## **ONSTAGE – 9:54 P.M.**

The band clicks in. **JIM** taps out that famous, regimented snare intro — march-like, relentless, perfect. The crowd hushes — even some of the airmen stop shouting.

**KATHY** kicks into the opening chord structure. Sparse. Icy. Controlled.

Then **BILL** enters.

A low, slow-building synth pad rises under it all — subtle and eerie, like a fog rolling in behind the battlefield. He dials in a distant warble, a ghostly echo, something that shouldn’t work but does.

**GILLEY** (singing, surprisingly restrained)
*I can't believe the news today…*

MRS. EVANS watches from the back, arms crossed, one eyebrow raised in maternal evaluation mode.

**GILLEY (CONT’D)**
*Oh, I can’t close my eyes and make it go away…*

**JEFF (V.O.)**
Somehow, we didn’t butcher it.

(beat)
**BILL** turned his synth into a wounded church organ. **JIM**’s snare cracked like gunfire.
**KATHY** played like she was holding it together with wire.

(beat)
And **GILLEY**, as always…sang like he meant it.

---
### **BACK OF THE ROOM**

Mrs. Evans watches the whole song. Doesn’t clap. Doesn’t react.  But she nods. Once.

**JOSLYN**
Is she…smiling?

**JOSIAH**
No. That’s her “I’m not mad, just surprised” face.

**MRS. EVANS**
They’re loud. But they’re not bad.

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – 9:58 P.M. – JULY 1990**

The crowd is buzzing—post-U2 hangover, beer-soaked and delighted, half-expecting anything.  Jim leaps to his feet behind the kit, sticks in the air, grin wide as a church.

**JIM** (announcing to the room)
Alright! You know what time it is!

(he thumps his chest, mispronounces)
PHIL COLLINS! “SUSSIDO!”

The crowd stirs—half the airmen cheer, a few groan, Kathy drops her head, mortified and giddy.

**GILLEY**
Jim, what the hell even is that?

**JIM**
“Sussidooo!” You know, that one! Ba da da da, da da—

**BILL**
(calmly correcting)
Sussudio, Jim. Sussudio. Like…the 80s exploded in a keyboard shop.

**JIM**
Whatever! Sussidooo! Let’s GO!

**KATHY**
I don’t even know the chords.

**JEFF**
Nobody knows the chords, Jim. Not even Phil Collins.

**JEREMY**
What key is it?

**BILL** (strapping in, possessed)
It’s in the key of “fuck it.”

He flips a switch. The synths *purr*—bass pad pounding, arpeggiator sparkling like Miami coke money.

Suddenly—
SFX: The classic “daaaa, duh-da-da-duh-dahhhh!” explodes over the bar, a wall of glorious, greasy sound.

**BILL**
Let’s ruin Phil Collins, Dayton-style.

**GILLEY**
That’s what he would’ve wanted. Probably.

**JIM**
ONE! TWO! THREE! FOUR!

The band *detonates* into a wild, off-script, half-remembered “Sussudio.”  Kathy makes up the chords. Jeremy fakes the changes. Gilley ad-libs every line. Jim tries to recreate the gated-reverb drum fill by sheer violence. Bill goes full mad scientist, dialing synth patches mid-song.

**THE CROWD:**
Three airmen are dancing on tables. Someone’s cousin attempts a moonwalk, slips in beer, keeps dancing. One woman is yelling “GENESIS 4EVER!” at the pool table, clearly thinking this is “*Invisible Touch*.”

Mrs. Evans, arms folded in the back, shakes her head, but even she cracks a smile.

**MRS. EVANS**
Now they’re just showing off.

---

**JEFF (V.O.)**
We didn’t know the words. We didn’t know the chords. We didn’t know the structure.  But for five minutes, we were Phil Collins.  And somewhere, Phil Collins was definitely confused.

(beat)
We made it through the set.  Somehow, we didn’t die.

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – STAGE – 10:07 P.M.**

The crowd is beyond drunk now. There’s that guy with no shirt making out with a jukebox, two airmen slow-dancing ironically, and Big Mike is actually smiling for once. Somewhere, a pool cue has snapped in half and no one cares.

**BILL** starts pounding out the iconic “*Take On Me*” synth riff, a wall of pure 1985 energy, thin and crisp and utterly perfect.

**KATHY** plays the chords, loose, precise. **JEREMY** and **JEFF** lay in the rhythm like a freight train made of hairspray and heartache.

And then? **GILLEY** leans into the mic.

**GILLEY** (softly, eyes locked on **KATHY**)
*Talking away…I don’t know what…I’m to say I’ll say it anyway…*

His voice is actually…kind of good? Raspy but tuneful, tender in all the wrong/right places.

But he doesn’t break eye contact.

**KATHY** (strumming, trying to breathe, failing)
Oh no.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
We thought it was just a bit. Just **GILLEY** being **GILLEY**. But he meant every word.

**GILLEY**
*Take on me…(take on me)*
*Take me on…(take on me)*
*I’ll be gone…*

He tilts his head. Smolders. He does a tiny little hip sway.

**GILLEY** **(CONT’D)**
*…in a day or two.*

**KATHY** (under her breath, panicked but impressed)
What the fuck is happening to me.

She fumbles a chord. Smiles despite herself. Bites her lip.

**JIM** (from the back)
Ohhhh my God it’s working.

**BILL**
This is a felony!

**JEFF**
I think I’m watching emotional arson.

**CROWD:**
CHEERING. SCREAMING. UNSURE WHY. MOVED.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
It wasn’t subtle. It wasn’t classy. But **GILLEY** gave **KATHY** everything he had in that one synth-drenched Norwegian love song.

(beat)
And the wild part? She gave it right back.

---

## **INT. W.O. WRIGHTS – BEHIND THE BAR – 1:02 A.M. – JULY 1990**

The lights are painfully bright. The air is soured by spilled beer, cold fries, and a night’s worth of human spillage.  The floor’s sticky, the crowd is gone—just a few stragglers arguing over change for the payphone, a single combat boot left under the bar, and the jukebox softly wheezing its way through “Free Bird.”

The band leans on the bar—wrecked, glowing, post-coital with victory and disbelief. Kathy’s got eyeliner running down her cheeks, Jeff’s shirt is inside out, Bill is clutching the synth like a baby, Jim is eating peanuts off the counter, Gilley’s shirt is still missing.

Big Mike lumbers out from the kitchen, hands rough, eyes softer than usual. He’s got a battered cash envelope and an ancient Folgers coffee can, heavy with coins.

**BIG MIKE**
Alright, listen up. You did it.

(beat)
Fifty bucks extra. No questions. You hit quota, you blew it out. You even got the airmen to scream. You didn’t fry the monitors, you didn’t break the bar, you didn’t puke on the jukebox. I call that a win.

He drops the envelope on the bar—creased, soft, a little damp.  Sets the coffee can beside it. The quarters inside *clunk* like treasure.

**BIG MIKE**
Half your bonus is in ones. The rest’s in quarters. Dart machine threw a fit. My loss, your laundry.

**KATHY** (wiping sweat, wide-eyed)
Are we supposed to split that?

**JIM**
Dibs on quarters. I got towels piling up.

**GILLEY**
Give me the ones. Nachos and Marlboros till sunrise.

**BIG MIKE**
Don’t spend it all at the arcade. Or do. Fuck it.

He leans in, dropping his voice.

**BIG MIKE**
Also?  You wanna come back? Got an opening two Thursdays from now. Some blues band lost a finger to a mower. Welcome to Dayton.

**JEFF**
That’s…unlucky.

**BIG MIKE**
Nah. That’s just Thursday.

(beat)
So, yes or no?

No hesitation. Kathy meets Gilley’s eyes. Jeremy looks at his hands. Bill grins, teeth pink with cherry cola.

**KATHY**
Yeah. We’re in.

**GILLEY**
Book us, Mike.

**BILL**
Can we bring the quarters?

**BIG MIKE**
Bring whatever the hell you had tonight. And spell your name right this time. I’m still not sure if you’re Commuted or Comuted.

**JEREMY**
We’re working on the branding. Slowly.

**BIG MIKE**
Keep it weird. Keep it loud. Bring people who drink and don’t get arrested.

He slides the envelope closer. Nods, once, like he’s just handed them the keys to the city. Then he disappears into the kitchen, already thinking about next Thursday’s fry order.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
The bar was empty. The neon was buzzing.  But for the first time, we weren’t.  We had quarters. We had a gig. We had a name—spelled right or wrong, it was ours.  And for a little while, that was enough.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S BEDROOM – 2:19 A.M. – JULY 1990**

The window A/C rattles like an old drum machine. Everything in the room glistens—cheap sweat, the glaze of aftershave, the halo of streetlamp slicing the dark in golden stripes. Kathy’s naked, legs splayed, thighs trembling as she rides Gilley, wet and wild and *grinding* for oblivion. Her hair sticks to her shoulders, lips parted, jaw tense. There’s no music, no laughter, just the hard slap of skin on skin and the guttural scrape of bed springs trying to outlast them.

She rides him like a dare, every motion deliberate, *punishing*, hips slamming down, thighs flexing, every inch of her body saying: Don’t think. Don’t feel. Just fuck.

**KATHY** (breathless, voice hoarse)
Don’t say a fucking word.

**GILLEY** (choked, barely there)
Wouldn’t dream of it.

His hands tremble—hovering over her waist, then clinging, trying to guide but really just holding on. He smells like cheap soap and the burnt sugar tang of whiskey sweat.  The old quilt slides off the edge of the bed, pooling on the floor. Their shoes are still by the door, laces tangled, a trail of clothes marking the stumble from midnight to this.

Kathy’s tits bounce, wild and unashamed, nipples shining, sweat running down her sternum, pooling at the small of her back. Every time she slams down, her breath tears out of her throat—a sound too raw to be pleasure, too desperate to be pain.

**KATHY** (tight, warning)
You don’t get to be smug about this.
(not looking at him)

**GILLEY** (gasping, half-laugh)
No smug left. Just trying to stay alive.

She grinds harder—faster, almost cruel. She won’t meet his eyes.  Her hands twist in the sheet above his head. Her back arches, but she never leans into him—never surrenders an inch. She’s not giving herself over; she’s taking what she needs.

Her mind is far away. He can feel it.  He wants to pull her down, to make her stay, to be the man she *wants*—but all he is tonight is the man she *needs* to forget whatever else burns in her.

**KATHY** (muttering, voice shaking)
Don’t moan. Don’t make it a story.

**GILLEY** (swallowing, voice rough)
Wasn’t planning on it.

A shudder passes through her—hips grinding, thighs slick, clit dragging over his pubic bone with each savage thrust. She’s not building to orgasm; she’s chasing it, chasing *something* that keeps skipping out of reach. Her face twists, teeth gritted. There’s hunger, but no joy.

**KATHY (V.O.)**
This should feel like a win. The after-party, the exclamation point. But it’s just static—like tuning past the good stations and landing on white noise.

She shakes her head, jaw locked, hair wild over her eyes.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
She’s been wet since the stage. For me, for the music, for the feeling of being seen and wanted and unbreakable. But now? Now it’s just bodies in a bed, trying to find the bottom of the night.

His hands drift—hips, ribs, under her ass—anywhere she’ll let him touch, but she never lets him hold her. She’s *on* him, not *with* him.

He stares at her—really sees her.  Her eyes glassy, her lips bitten bloody, her tits bouncing with a violence that’s more distraction than invitation. Her whole body is right here—but her heart, her mind, her need is somewhere else. With someone else.

He thinks about saying something. He doesn’t.  Instead, he lets her ride him, lets her use him, lets the night burn itself out between them. The bed squeals. The streetlight paints stripes on her back. They fuck like they’re the last two people left, like they know they’ll never talk about this again.

And when it’s over?  It’s not relief.  It’s not triumph.  It’s just silence.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
This is how bad decisions start: not with a kiss, not with a song—but with a need so sharp you can’t tell if it’s pleasure or punishment.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S BEDROOM – 2:49 A.M.**

Silence again.

**KATHY** is sprawled across the bed, limbs splayed, hair damp, skin cooling under the lazy swipes of a broken ceiling fan. Her chest rises and falls slowly, rhythmically. Spent.
**GILLEY** lies beside her, flat on his back, one arm tucked behind his head, the other reaching down under the sheets.

He winces slightly. Then casually peels off the condom, no ceremony, no thought, and tosses it toward the wastebasket.  It *misses*, landing with a quiet, wet slap on top of a spiral notebook next to his amp.

**CAMERA CLOSE-UP:
** The notebook — smudged, bent, pages dog-eared — reads *“Setlists / **KATHY**’s Riffs – DO NOT TOUCH”* in **KATHY**’s handwriting.

He doesn’t notice. Or doesn’t care.

**GILLEY** (low)
 So.

(beat)
You realize what we did tonight?

**KATHY** (sighs)
Fucked?

**GILLEY** (smiling)
Besides that.

(beat)

We killed it. W.O. Wright’s. We actually pulled it off.

**KATHY**
I know. Don’t remind me. I’m already bracing for the emotional crash.

**GILLEY** 
That crowd? That noise? We held it. Kept it. Made those bastards stay.

(beat)
Big Mike handed me a can full of quarters like it was a goddamn Grammy.

**KATHY** 
Hey, that can pay for at least one Mountain Dew and two Slim Jims. We’re basically famous.

**GILLEY** 
I’m serious, though.

(beat)
We weren’t just okay. We were…good.   *Better* than we had any right to be.

**KATHY** (quietly)
Yeah. We were.

(beat)
Not polished. Not tight. But alive.

**GILLEY**
Exactly.

(beat)

There was a moment, when we were halfway through *“Sunday Bloody Sunday”*  I looked out, and for like, two seconds? Everyone in that room was locked in. Actually listening. Not just drinking. Not just waiting. Like…in it.

**KATHY** 
I saw it too.

(beat)
Thought I was imagining it.

**GILLEY** 
We gave ‘em something they didn’t expect.

**KATHY** 
Which, in that bar, is saying something.

(beat)
I think we have to do it again.

**GILLEY** 
We will. Two weeks.

(beat)
You in?

**KATHY** 
Of course I’m in.

She rolls away from him, turning to her side. Not cold. Not angry. Just done with the Moment — with *this* Moment.

**KATHY** (CONT’D)
But next time?  Let’s maybe not do Phil Collins again.

**GILLEY**(deep breath)
No promises.

A long beat.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
They wouldn’t talk about the sex.  Not tomorrow. Not next week.  Maybe never.  But that night?  They both knew it wasn’t about love.  It was about making something loud.

**CAMERA PUSHES IN on the notebook on the floor.**  A string of ink smeared from the used condom draped across the cover.

Something important, now marked. And soon, remembered.

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S KITCHEN – MORNING – 9:06 A.M. – JULY 1990**

Harsh sun, spilled coffee, and the sense that even the linoleum is judging them. Kathy’s standing at the counter, dressed for battle, the “#1 Dad” mug burning her palm and her pride. Last night’s eyeliner is war paint, defiance and exhaustion cut in black under her eyes.

Gilley shuffles in, shirtless, bruised from sleep and sex and the realization that nothing in this room will ever be the same. He smiles, a half-broken try for normal.

She kills it instantly.

**KATHY**
We can’t keep fucking.

It’s not just a line. It’s a division bell.  Gilley sags, the hope leaking out of him like pre-cum, arms slack, one hand reaching for the fridge out of muscle memory.

**GILLEY**
…Okay.

She doesn’t flinch.

**KATHY**
No games. No will-they-won’t-they. Just, no more.

Her voice is tired, not angry. Honest, not cruel. Like someone tending to a wound.

**KATHY** (CONT’D)
I’m not gonna be the girl who sleeps with her bandmate because the gig went well.

Gilley stares at the floor. Tries for a joke, fails.  She keeps going.

**KATHY** (CONT’D)
I think I used you like a smoke alarm I could finally rip off the wall. Just to stop the noise in my head for one night.

He blinks. Wants to protest, but she’s right.  She won’t let him shrink the moment.

**KATHY** (CONT’D)
Don’t try to make this about last night’s reaction. Don’t try to tidy it up so you can put it back on the shelf.

A long silence.

They both remember the show, the sweat, the condom flung across her notebook. Everything is evidence now.  She drains her mug, slams it down hard, ceramic cracks spiderwebbing up the side.

**KATHY**
I’ll see you at practice.

She walks out, boots loud on the tile.  Doesn’t look back.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
She thought she was saving the band.  Saving herself. Maybe even me. But all I felt was the shape of her leaving. The shape of her ass?  A door I thought would stay open just a little longer.

He stands, alone, in the kitchen.  A mug sweating on the counter, the smell of old sex and new loneliness curling in the air.

---

## **EXT. GILLEY’S FRONT PORCH – CONTINUOUS**

Kathy’s halfway to her car.  The door slams open behind her.

Gilley—bare-chested, eyes raw—flings her bra out onto the grass.

**GILLEY**
You forgot your fucking bra!

It sails, limp, lands in the dew like a surrender flag.  She keeps walking.  He can’t stand it.

**GILLEY** (CONT’D)
You fucking *cunt!*

The word lands. Too sharp. Too loud.  A neighbor’s dog barks, then falls silent. The world stops breathing.

Kathy stops.  Stands still, stiff as stone. Turns. Walks back.

She picks up the bra, two fingers, surgical, like she’s picking up a rattlesnake. Walks up to the porch, stands close enough for him to feel the chill radiating off her.

**KATHY**
You don’t get to call me that. Not onstage. Not off. Not ever.

(beat)
Grow up, Gilley.

She keeps the bra.  She walks away, boots biting gravel, and doesn’t look back.  Gilley stays in the doorway, shaking, fists clenched, chest bare to the wind and the consequences.

**JEFF (V.O.)**
That was the moment he stopped being the dreamer and became the actor.

---

# **CHAPTER FIVE : COUNTING COUP**

---

## **INT. GILLEY’S BEDROOM – 9:42 A.M. – JULY 1990**

Silence, sunlight, the ghost of last night’s sex heavy in the air. Kathy’s perfume still rides the edge of the pillow, but the room doesn’t mourn her. It’s *ready for the next act.*

Gilley’s sitting in his boxers, grinning, legs wide, sprawled across the bed like a king who survived the revolution. The sheets are tangled and stained. Her bra’s gone, her absence is just a challenge, a vacancy.

He digs under a pile of crumpled jeans, receipts, and crushed Marlboro packs, and finds what he’s looking for:  A battered spiral notepad.

*Names / Numbers / Maybe.* Written bold in Sharpie. There are lipstick marks. Perfume. Little hearts. Checkmarks, exclamation points, phone numbers running into the margins.

He flips through, almost whistling, savoring the memory of each conquest, each name.

- **Angie** – Blonde, cousin’s wedding, never said no to a dare.
- **Trina** – Beer rep’s niece, been eyeing him since summer, laughed at every joke.
- “**??? – Waffle House?**” – No idea, but the memory’s sweet.
- **Shelly** – Darts night, sang harmonies, wanted more than he could give.
- **Stacey** – Airman groupie, camo jacket, a legend in the making.
- **“Not KATHY”** – underlined, twice, circled in anger and longing.

He tears out “Not KATHY.”  Lets it drop into the ashtray like an old habit he’s finally giving up tomorrow.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
Six numbers. Six maybes. Six shots at waking up a winner.

(beat)
You can lose a girl and still win the day.

He lands on Trina’s name. Her handwriting is bubbly, and he can almost hear her voice in his ear—last week, flirting by the amps, eyes big, lips parted, *waiting for him to call*.

He doesn’t hesitate.  Dials the number with one hand, already imagining the next round.

**GILLEY** (smirking, phone to ear)
Hey, Trina.

Yeah, it’s Gilley—from the show.

Nah, I’m not busy. What are you doing right now?

He grins as she answers—immediate, eager, no playing hard to get. He laughs, low and cocky.

**GILLEY (V.O.)**
That’s the thing about being me. Doors don’t close. They spin.

He hangs up. Tosses the notepad aside. He’s got options—he always does.  But then—he pauses, flips back, and finds the gold medal: **Stacey Dawson**.  Big, round handwriting. A phone number with two hearts.  She’s the one everyone bets against. The one even *he* isn’t sure he can pull.  She’s married—sort of. To Jeremy Dawson, technically.  But he knows the look. The secret. The dare.  He says her name out loud, letting it roll off his tongue like a promise.
